\section{Local Uniqueness and Stability Program for the TECT Flavor Branch}

\section{Short explanation}

At this stage, the correct next task is not further heuristic fitting.
It is to determine whether the phenomenologically successful branch is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a locally unique stationary point of the reduced TECT free energy,
\item dynamically stable under the linearized evolution,
\item stable or attractive under the relevant RG flow in the reduced parameter sector.
\end{enumerate}

The strongest honest statement currently available is existence of a phenomenologically viable branch, not yet a full proof of uniqueness.

\section{1. Correct status statement}

Let
\[
\mathcal P=(\ell,x,y,\theta_0,\kappa,\ldots)
\]
denote the reduced branch parameters.
The current numerical/analytic work supports the existence of a branch
\[
\mathcal P=\mathcal P_*
\]
such that the derived Yukawa/CKM observables are phenomenologically viable.

However, existence does not imply:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{global uniqueness},
\qquad
\text{global dynamical selection},
\qquad
\text{or RG attractiveness}.
}
\label{eq:stage8_status}
\end{equation}

So the correct next problem is a local uniqueness and stability analysis around \(\mathcal P_*\).

\section{2. Reduced free-energy functional}

Let the reduced effective free energy after shell truncation and defect reduction be
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{red}}(\mathcal P)
=
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{Braz}}(\ell;\mu,Z,Y,g)
+
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{def}}(\theta_0,\kappa)
+
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{geom}}(\ell,x,y)
+
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{mix}}(\mathcal P).
}
\label{eq:stage8_Fred}
\end{equation}

The precise formulas may vary with truncation, but the local analysis depends only on smoothness near a candidate point \(\mathcal P_*\).

A stationary branch satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\nabla_{\mathcal P}\mathcal F_{\mathrm{red}}(\mathcal P_*)=0.
}
\label{eq:stage8_stationary}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Local uniqueness criterion}

Define the reduced Hessian
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_{IJ}(\mathcal P_*)
:=
\frac{\partial^2\mathcal F_{\mathrm{red}}}{\partial \mathcal P_I\,\partial \mathcal P_J}
\Bigg|_{\mathcal P=\mathcal P_*}.
}
\label{eq:stage8_Hessian}
\end{equation}

Then the standard implicit-function / Morse-type local uniqueness criterion is:

\paragraph{Local uniqueness criterion.}
If
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det H(\mathcal P_*)\neq 0,
}
\label{eq:stage8_nondeg}
\end{equation}
then \(\mathcal P_*\) is an isolated stationary point.

Moreover, if
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H(\mathcal P_*)>0
\quad\text{(positive definite)},
}
\label{eq:stage8_posdef}
\end{equation}
then \(\mathcal P_*\) is a strict local minimum and therefore locally unique among nearby stationary points.

So the first rigorous task is not “more fitting” but:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{compute the reduced Hessian }H(\mathcal P_*)\text{ and its eigenvalues.}
}
\label{eq:stage8_first_task}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Separation of shape and scale directions}

Because earlier stages exhibited scale redundancies, one must compute the Hessian only on the genuinely reduced parameter space.
Let
\[
\mathcal P=(\mathcal P_{\mathrm{shape}},\mathcal P_{\mathrm{scale}})
\]
with the scale directions already quotiented out or fixed analytically.

Then the correct uniqueness object is the projected Hessian
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{phys}}
=
\Pi_{\mathrm{phys}}^T\,H\,\Pi_{\mathrm{phys}},
}
\label{eq:stage8_Hphys}
\end{equation}
where \(\Pi_{\mathrm{phys}}\) projects onto the nonredundant tangent space.

A zero mode of the full Hessian may be harmless if it lies entirely in a gauge/redundancy direction.
What matters is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{phys}}>0.
}
\label{eq:stage8_Hphyspos}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Linear dynamical stability}

Let the reduced dynamics near the branch be written schematically as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ddot{\delta\mathcal P}
+
\Gamma\,\dot{\delta\mathcal P}
+
L\,\delta\mathcal P
=0,
}
\label{eq:stage8_linear_dyn}
\end{equation}
where
\[
L=\left.\frac{\partial^2 \mathcal F_{\mathrm{red}}}{\partial \mathcal P^2}\right|_{\mathcal P_*}
\]
up to normalization/kinetic factors.

If the kinetic metric \(K_{\mathrm{red}}\) is positive definite, the linear mode frequencies are determined by the generalized eigenvalue problem
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L\,v=\omega^2 K_{\mathrm{red}}\,v.
}
\label{eq:stage8_gen_eig}
\end{equation}

The branch is linearly stable if
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\omega_a^2>0
\quad\text{for all physical modes }a.
}
\label{eq:stage8_linear_stable}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, if \(K_{\mathrm{red}}>0\), this reduces to positivity of the physical Hessian:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{phys}}>0
\Longrightarrow
\text{linear stability.}
}
\label{eq:stage8_H_implies_stab}
\end{equation}

So local uniqueness and linear stability are tightly linked.

\section{6. RG stability in the reduced flavor sector}

Now let \(g_I\) denote the reduced couplings controlling the flavor/anisotropy branch, for example
\[
g_I\in\{\gamma,\phi,\Delta_i^{(u)},\Delta_i^{(d)},\ldots\}.
\]

Suppose their RG flow is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d g_I}{d\ell}
=
\beta_I(g).
}
\label{eq:stage8_beta}
\end{equation}

A reduced fixed branch \(g_*\) satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta_I(g_*)=0.
}
\label{eq:stage8_fixed}
\end{equation}

Define the stability matrix
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M_{IJ}
:=
\frac{\partial \beta_I}{\partial g_J}\Bigg|_{g=g_*}.
}
\label{eq:stage8_M}
\end{equation}

Then:

\begin{itemize}
\item if all eigenvalues of \(M\) have negative real part, the branch is RG-attractive;
\item if some are positive, the branch is RG-repulsive along those directions;
\item if some vanish, nonlinear terms must be included.
\end{itemize}

Thus the RG question is mathematically separate from the static uniqueness question.

\section{7. What can already be claimed honestly?}

At the present stage, the honest strongest claim is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{there exists a phenomenologically viable reduced branch } \mathcal P_*,
}
\label{eq:stage8_exists}
\end{equation}
and there are structural reasons to expect it to be locally stable, but
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{local uniqueness is not yet proven until }H_{\mathrm{phys}}\text{ is computed,}
}
\label{eq:stage8_notproven1}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{RG attractiveness is not yet proven until }M\text{ is computed.}
}
\label{eq:stage8_notproven2}
\end{equation}

This is the correct boundary between proven and unproven statements.

\section{8. Minimal rigorous program to close the branch}

Therefore the minimal rigorous closure program is:

\paragraph{Step A.}
Compute the reduced physical Hessian
\[
H_{\mathrm{phys}}(\mathcal P_*).
\]

\paragraph{Step B.}
Check whether
\[
H_{\mathrm{phys}}>0.
\]

\paragraph{Step C.}
Compute the reduced kinetic metric \(K_{\mathrm{red}}\) and verify it is positive definite.

\paragraph{Step D.}
Construct the reduced beta functions \(\beta_I(g)\) for the flavor-relevant couplings.

\paragraph{Step E.}
Evaluate the RG stability matrix \(M\) and its eigenvalues.

Only after Steps A--E can one honestly upgrade “successful branch exists” to “successful branch is locally selected and stable.”

\section{9. Concrete reduced Hessian variables}

For the immediately relevant reduced branch, the first practical Hessian may be taken on
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal P_{\mathrm{red}}
=
(\ell,x,y,\theta_0,\kappa,\phi)
}
\label{eq:stage8_Pred}
\end{equation}
or, if \(\phi\) is already integrated out geometrically,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal P_{\mathrm{red}}
=
(\ell,x,y,\theta_0,\kappa).
}
\label{eq:stage8_Pred2}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{phys}}
=
\left[
\frac{\partial^2\mathcal F_{\mathrm{red}}}{\partial \mathcal P_I \partial \mathcal P_J}
\right]_{\mathcal P_*}.
}
\label{eq:stage8_Hfinal}
\end{equation}

This is a finite \(5\times5\) or \(6\times6\) matrix problem.
So the problem is no longer conceptual; it is computationally finite.

\section{10. Honest endpoint}

The correct next sentence is not:
\[
\text{``TECT flavor is fully derived.''}
\]

The correct sentence is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT currently supports a viable and structured flavor branch,}
}
\label{eq:stage8_final1}
\end{equation}
and the remaining mathematical frontier is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{local uniqueness + dynamical stability + RG attractiveness of that branch.}
}
\label{eq:stage8_final2}
\end{equation}

This is a much stronger and more trustworthy position than overclaiming global completion.

\section{Final Summary}

The correct next step is to replace overstrong completion claims by a rigorous local uniqueness and stability program.

Let \(\mathcal P_*\) denote a phenomenologically viable reduced flavor branch.
Then the correct mathematical questions are:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Is } \mathcal P_* \text{ a locally unique stationary point?}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Is } \mathcal P_* \text{ linearly dynamically stable?}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Is } \mathcal P_* \text{ RG-attractive?}
}
\]

The local uniqueness criterion is determined by the reduced physical Hessian
\[
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{phys}}
=
\Pi_{\mathrm{phys}}^T
\left[
\frac{\partial^2\mathcal F_{\mathrm{red}}}{\partial \mathcal P^2}
\right]_{\mathcal P_*}
\Pi_{\mathrm{phys}}.
}
\]
If \(H_{\mathrm{phys}}>0\), then the branch is a strict local minimum and hence locally unique.

Linear dynamical stability is determined by the generalized eigenvalue problem
\[
\boxed{
L v=\omega^2 K_{\mathrm{red}} v,
}
\]
and follows if \(K_{\mathrm{red}}>0\) and \(H_{\mathrm{phys}}>0\).

RG stability is determined by the reduced beta-function Jacobian
\[
\boxed{
M_{IJ}=\partial \beta_I/\partial g_J|_{g_*}.
}
\]
The branch is RG-attractive only if all eigenvalues of \(M\) have negative real part.

Therefore the honest current status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a viable TECT flavor branch exists,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{its local uniqueness, dynamical stability, and RG attractiveness are still the remaining finite matrix problems to be solved.}
}
\]

So the immediate frontier is the explicit computation of
\[
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{phys}},\qquad K_{\mathrm{red}},\qquad M.
}
\]

\section{Experimental Connection Strategy for the Current TECT Flavor Branch}

\section{Short explanation}

At the present stage, the correct experimental strategy is not to advertise a full Standard-Model derivation.
It is to formulate the current TECT flavor branch as a low-dimensional, falsifiable set of correlated predictions.

The right experimental target is therefore an \emph{over-constrained branch test} in the quark sector.

\section{1. Observable map of the current branch}

Let the reduced branch parameters be denoted schematically by
\[
\mathcal P_{\mathrm{flav}}
=
(\ell,x,y,\gamma,\phi,\Delta^{(u)}_i,\Delta^{(d)}_i,\ldots),
\]
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \((\ell,x,y)\) encode the effective family geometry;
\item \(\gamma\) encodes the anisotropic metric deformation;
\item \(\phi\) is the effective CP/Berry phase;
\item \(\Delta_i^{(u,d)}\) encode right-handed hierarchical suppressions.
\end{itemize}

Then the current reduced TECT branch predicts
\[
Y_u(\mathcal P_{\mathrm{flav}}),\qquad
Y_d(\mathcal P_{\mathrm{flav}}),
\]
and therefore the observable set
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{quark}}
=
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|,J
\right\}.
}
\label{eq:expconn_quark_obs}
\end{equation}

The key experimental point is that these are \emph{not} independent outputs:
they are correlated through a low-dimensional parameter branch.

\section{2. The correct experimental test: over-constraint, not postdiction}

The correct immediate test is to fit a subset of observables and predict the rest.

A clean version is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Fit } 
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|
\right\}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{predict }
\{|V_{ub}|,J\}.
}
\label{eq:expconn_overconstraint}
\end{equation}

This is much stronger than fitting everything simultaneously.
If the branch survives this test, it becomes genuinely predictive.

\section{3. Useful branch-level correlations}

Even before full likelihood fitting, the current reduced structure implies the following schematic relations.

First, the small CKM element is controlled by the longest effective family separation and the CP-sensitive interference sector:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\log |V_{ub}|
\sim
-\frac14\Big(d_{13,G}^2-d_{23,G}^2\Big)
+
\Delta_{\phi},
}
\label{eq:expconn_vub}
\end{equation}
where
\[
d_{AB,G}^2:=(q_A-q_B)^T G (q_A-q_B),
\]
and \(\Delta_\phi\) summarizes the phase-interference correction.

Second, the Jarlskog invariant is constrained by the same single branch:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J
\sim
|V_{us}|\,|V_{cb}|\,|V_{ub}|\,\sin\delta_{\mathrm{CKM}},
}
\label{eq:expconn_J}
\end{equation}
with \(\delta_{\mathrm{CKM}}\) itself not independent in the branch picture, but induced by the effective Berry/topological phase parameter \(\phi\).

Therefore the branch is most efficiently falsified by the pair
\[
(|V_{ub}|,J),
\]
once the larger magnitudes and mass ratios are fixed.

\section{4. Immediate experimental arena: flavour factories and heavy-flavour data}

The present quark-sector branch should be confronted first with the precision flavour programme.

Belle II is explicitly designed for precise weak-interaction parameter measurements and, as of December 2024, had accumulated a total sample of \(575\,\mathrm{fb}^{-1}\).
Thus Belle II is already in the correct regime for precision flavour over-constraint tests.

At CERN, Run 3 had already surpassed the total integrated luminosity of Run 2 by September 2024, and the same report states that \(7.6\,\mathrm{fb}^{-1}\) had been delivered to LHCb by 2 September 2024.
So the hadron-machine flavour programme is also in the relevant statistical regime.

Hence the immediate experimental programme is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT branch} \quad \leftrightarrow \quad
\text{Belle II + LHC flavour over-constraint test}
}
\label{eq:expconn_immediate}
\end{equation}

using tree-dominated CKM observables as the cleanest first arena.

\section{5. The correct fit architecture}

The first experimental likelihood should be organized as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm{exp}}
=
\chi^2_{\mathrm{masses}}
+
\chi^2_{\mathrm{CKM,large}}
+
\chi^2_{\mathrm{pred}}
}
\label{eq:expconn_chi2}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\chi^2_{\mathrm{masses}}
\ \text{for}\ 
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b}
\right\},
\]
\[
\chi^2_{\mathrm{CKM,large}}
\ \text{for}\ 
\{|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|\},
\]
and
\[
\chi^2_{\mathrm{pred}}
\ \text{for the out-of-sample tests}\ 
\{|V_{ub}|,J\}.
\]

This prevents the branch from succeeding merely by overfitting all observables simultaneously.

\section{6. Honest status of lepton-sector experimental connection}

A lepton-sector extension is important but should be kept logically separate.

NOvA's 2024 update doubled the dataset relative to its previous release, improved the oscillation precision, and favored normal ordering more strongly, but still left the mass-ordering/CP ambiguity unresolved.

DUNE's 2025 status presentation states that for maximal \(\delta_{CP}\) it expects \(>3\sigma\) sensitivity within 3.5 years, and for 75\% of \(\delta_{CP}\) values in about 14 years.

Therefore the honest lepton-sector statement is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{DUNE/NOvA provide the right future arena for a TECT lepton-phase extension,}
}
\label{eq:expconn_lepton_future}
\end{equation}
but
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the currently mature experimental target is the quark flavour branch.}
}
\label{eq:expconn_lepton_notyet}
\end{equation}

\section{7. What would count as a genuine success?}

The current TECT branch should be considered experimentally nontrivial only if:

\begin{enumerate}
\item one reduced parameter branch fits the mass ratios and large CKM entries;
\item the same branch predicts \(|V_{ub}|\) and \(J\) within the experimental uncertainty band;
\item the prediction is robust under small parameter perturbations and does not require severe retuning.
\end{enumerate}

So the correct success criterion is not “a fit exists,” but rather
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the same low-dimensional branch survives an out-of-sample flavour test.}
}
\label{eq:expconn_success}
\end{equation}

\section{8. Honest conclusion}

The correct current experimental message is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT is ready for a quark-sector over-constraint test now,}
}
\label{eq:expconn_now}
\end{equation}
using precision flavour data from the current Belle II and LHC flavour programmes,

while
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the lepton-sector CP test belongs to the next extension stage, with DUNE as the natural long-baseline arena.}
}
\label{eq:expconn_dune}
\end{equation}

This is the most rigorous and falsifiable experimental bridge from the current theory status.

\section{Final Summary}

The correct experimental bridge for the current TECT flavour branch is not a vague “fit to everything,” but a falsifiable over-constraint test.

The present quark-sector branch predicts the correlated observable set
\[
\boxed{
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|,J
\right\}.
}
\]

The correct immediate test is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{fit }
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|
\right\}
\ \Longrightarrow\
\text{predict }
\{|V_{ub}|,J\}.
}
\]

This should be confronted with the current precision flavour programme.
Belle II is already operating in the relevant regime, with a total sample of \(575\,\mathrm{fb}^{-1}\) reported as of December 2024, and the current LHC Run 3 programme has already exceeded Run 2 integrated luminosity, with \(7.6\,\mathrm{fb}^{-1}\) delivered to LHCb by 2 September 2024. :contentReference[oaicite:8]{index=8}

So the immediate experimental target is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT quark flavour branch} \leftrightarrow \text{Belle II + LHC flavour over-constraint test.}
}
\]

The lepton-sector extension should be treated separately.
NOvA’s 2024 update improved oscillation precision but still left the ordering/CP ambiguity unresolved, while DUNE’s 2025 status projects \(>3\sigma\) CP-violation sensitivity in 3.5 years for maximal \(\delta_{CP}\), and in about 14 years for 75\% of \(\delta_{CP}\) values. :contentReference[oaicite:9]{index=9}

Therefore the honest current status is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT is ready now for a quark-sector flavour falsification test,}
}
\]
while
\[
\boxed{
\text{its lepton-sector CP programme belongs to the next extension stage.}
}
\]

\section{Experimental Over-Constraint Likelihood for the TECT Quark Branch}

\section{Short explanation}

The correct experimental test of the current TECT quark branch is not a simultaneous fit to all observables.
It is an \emph{over-constrained} fit:
a subset of observables is used to calibrate the branch, and the remaining observables are predicted out-of-sample.

This turns the branch from a descriptive fit into a falsifiable theory test.

\section{1. Theory-to-data map}

Let the reduced TECT branch parameters be denoted by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Theta
=
(\ell,x,y,\gamma,\phi,\Delta_i^{(u)},\Delta_i^{(d)},\ldots).
}
\label{eq:stage10_theta}
\end{equation}

The branch determines the Yukawa matrices
\[
Y_u(\Theta),\qquad Y_d(\Theta),
\]
and therefore the derived observables
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{th}}(\Theta;\mu_{\rm ref})
=
\left\{
R_u^{(1)},R_u^{(2)},R_d^{(1)},R_d^{(2)},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|,J
\right\}_{\mathrm{th}},
}
\label{eq:stage10_otheory}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_u^{(1)}:=\left.\frac{m_u}{m_t}\right|_{\mu_{\rm ref}},
\qquad
R_u^{(2)}:=\left.\frac{m_c}{m_t}\right|_{\mu_{\rm ref}},
}
\label{eq:stage10_Ru}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_d^{(1)}:=\left.\frac{m_d}{m_b}\right|_{\mu_{\rm ref}},
\qquad
R_d^{(2)}:=\left.\frac{m_s}{m_b}\right|_{\mu_{\rm ref}}.
}
\label{eq:stage10_Rd}
\end{equation}

The common renormalization scale \(\mu_{\rm ref}\) must be fixed before any numerical comparison is meaningful.

\section{2. Experimental observable split}

Define the \emph{fit bucket}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{fit}}
=
\left\{
R_u^{(1)},R_u^{(2)},R_d^{(1)},R_d^{(2)},|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|
\right\},
}
\label{eq:stage10_fitbucket}
\end{equation}
and the \emph{prediction bucket}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{pred}}
=
\left\{
|V_{ub}|,J
\right\}.
}
\label{eq:stage10_predbucket}
\end{equation}

This split is chosen because:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the mass ratios and larger CKM entries are the natural branch-calibration observables;
\item \(|V_{ub}|\) and \(J\) are the most sensitive small quantities and therefore the sharpest falsifiers.
\end{enumerate}

\section{3. Covariance structure}

Let
\[
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{fit}}^{\mathrm{exp}},\qquad
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{pred}}^{\mathrm{exp}}
\]
denote the experimental central values at the same reference scheme/scale convention.

Let
\[
C_{\mathrm{fit}}^{\mathrm{exp}},\qquad
C_{\mathrm{pred}}^{\mathrm{exp}}
\]
be the corresponding experimental covariance matrices.

The theory side also has uncertainty from truncation, branch reduction, and scale matching.
So define
\[
C_{\mathrm{fit}}^{\mathrm{th}},\qquad
C_{\mathrm{pred}}^{\mathrm{th}},
\]
and the total covariances
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_{\mathrm{fit}}=C_{\mathrm{fit}}^{\mathrm{exp}}+C_{\mathrm{fit}}^{\mathrm{th}},
\qquad
C_{\mathrm{pred}}=C_{\mathrm{pred}}^{\mathrm{exp}}+C_{\mathrm{pred}}^{\mathrm{th}}.
}
\label{eq:stage10_cov_total}
\end{equation}

This is essential: without \(C^{\mathrm{th}}\), one overstates the precision of the current branch.

\section{4. Calibration likelihood}

Define the calibration residual
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta)
=
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{fit}}^{\mathrm{th}}(\Theta)
-
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{fit}}^{\mathrm{exp}}.
}
\label{eq:stage10_dfit}
\end{equation}

Then the calibration likelihood is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta)
=
\Delta_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta)^T
C_{\mathrm{fit}}^{-1}
\Delta_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta).
}
\label{eq:stage10_chifit}
\end{equation}

The best-fit branch point is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Theta_*=\arg\min_{\Theta}\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta).
}
\label{eq:stage10_theta_star}
\end{equation}

Crucially, \(|V_{ub}|\) and \(J\) are \emph{not} included in this minimization.

\section{5. Prediction likelihood}

At the best-fit branch point \(\Theta_*\), define the prediction residual
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_{\mathrm{pred}}(\Theta_*)
=
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{pred}}^{\mathrm{th}}(\Theta_*)
-
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{pred}}^{\mathrm{exp}}.
}
\label{eq:stage10_dpred}
\end{equation}

Then the over-constraint test statistic is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm{pred}}(\Theta_*)
=
\Delta_{\mathrm{pred}}(\Theta_*)^T
C_{\mathrm{pred}}^{-1}
\Delta_{\mathrm{pred}}(\Theta_*).
}
\label{eq:stage10_chipred}
\end{equation}

This is the central quantity that determines whether the calibrated branch genuinely predicts the out-of-sample observables.

\section{6. Viable branch region and predicted intervals}

The correct test should not rely on a single point only.
Define the \(\alpha\)-confidence viable branch set
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal V_\alpha
=
\left\{
\Theta:
\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta)
\le
\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta_*)+\Delta_\alpha
\right\},
}
\label{eq:stage10_valpha}
\end{equation}
where \(\Delta_\alpha\) is the appropriate chi-square threshold for the number of fitted parameters.

Then the theory prediction interval for any predicted observable \(X\in\{|V_{ub}|,J\}\) is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
X_{\min,\alpha}
=
\min_{\Theta\in\mathcal V_\alpha} X^{\mathrm{th}}(\Theta),
\qquad
X_{\max,\alpha}
=
\max_{\Theta\in\mathcal V_\alpha} X^{\mathrm{th}}(\Theta).
}
\label{eq:stage10_pred_interval}
\end{equation}

This is much more honest than quoting a single central prediction.

\section{7. Stronger falsification criterion}

A branch should be considered genuinely predictive only if the out-of-sample observables lie in a narrow interval after calibration.

So define the normalized prediction widths
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_{V_{ub}}
=
\frac{|V_{ub}|_{\max,\alpha}-|V_{ub}|_{\min,\alpha}}
{|V_{ub}|^{\mathrm{exp}}},
\qquad
W_J
=
\frac{J_{\max,\alpha}-J_{\min,\alpha}}{|J^{\mathrm{exp}}|}.
}
\label{eq:stage10_widths}
\end{equation}

Then a strong predictive branch should satisfy
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_{V_{ub}}\ll 1,
\qquad
W_J\ll 1.
}
\label{eq:stage10_strong_pred}
\end{equation}

If these widths are huge, the branch may fit but is not yet predictive.

\section{8. Practical likelihood variants}

There are two proper ways to implement the quark masses.

\subsection{Variant A: direct running-mass ratios}
Use
\[
R_u^{(1)},R_u^{(2)},R_d^{(1)},R_d^{(2)}
\]
directly at the chosen common scale \(\mu_{\rm ref}\).

\subsection{Variant B: log-mass coordinates}
Because hierarchical masses vary across many orders of magnitude, it is numerically better to define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
X_u^{(1)}:=\log R_u^{(1)},
\quad
X_u^{(2)}:=\log R_u^{(2)},
\quad
X_d^{(1)}:=\log R_d^{(1)},
\quad
X_d^{(2)}:=\log R_d^{(2)}.
}
\label{eq:stage10_logmass}
\end{equation}

Then
\[
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{fit}}
=
\{X_u^{(1)},X_u^{(2)},X_d^{(1)},X_d^{(2)},|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|\},
\]
which usually leads to a better conditioned fit.

\section{9. Recommended practical test}

The most rigorous near-term TECT quark-sector test is therefore:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Fit }
\{X_u^{(1)},X_u^{(2)},X_d^{(1)},X_d^{(2)},|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|\}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{predict }
\{|V_{ub}|,J\}.
}
\label{eq:stage10_recommended}
\end{equation}

This is the correct branch-level experimental test because it forces the same low-dimensional geometry/topology branch to survive the most delicate observables.

\section{10. Honest interpretation rules}

The correct interpretation is:

\begin{itemize}
\item if \(\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}\) is bad, the branch is immediately ruled out;
\item if \(\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}\) is good but \(\chi^2_{\mathrm{pred}}\) is bad, the branch is descriptive but not predictive;
\item if both are good and \(W_{V_{ub}},W_J\ll1\), then the branch becomes genuinely predictive.
\end{itemize}

Thus the key test is not existence of one tuned point, but survival of an over-constrained likelihood with narrow predicted intervals.

\section{11. Honest endpoint}

At this stage, the correct next deliverable is not another qualitative argument.
It is the explicit likelihood scan
\[
\Theta \mapsto \chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta),
\]
followed by the prediction intervals for \(|V_{ub}|\) and \(J\).

Only that scan can tell us whether the current TECT quark branch is merely viable or already sharply predictive.

\section{Final Summary}

The correct experimental test of the current TECT quark branch is an over-constrained likelihood split into a calibration bucket and a prediction bucket.

At a common renormalization scale \(\mu_{\rm ref}\), define the theory observable vector
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{th}}(\Theta;\mu_{\rm ref})
=
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|,J
\right\}_{\mathrm{th}}.
}
\]

The proper split is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{fit}}
=
\left\{
\log\frac{m_u}{m_t},
\log\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\log\frac{m_d}{m_b},
\log\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|
\right\},
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{pred}}
=
\{|V_{ub}|,J\}.
}
\]

The calibration likelihood is
\[
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta)
=
\Delta_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta)^T
C_{\mathrm{fit}}^{-1}
\Delta_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta),
}
\]
and the branch point is
\[
\boxed{
\Theta_*=\arg\min_\Theta \chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta).
}
\]

The out-of-sample prediction test is
\[
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm{pred}}(\Theta_*)
=
\Delta_{\mathrm{pred}}(\Theta_*)^T
C_{\mathrm{pred}}^{-1}
\Delta_{\mathrm{pred}}(\Theta_*).
}
\]

The viable branch region is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal V_\alpha
=
\left\{
\Theta:
\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta)\le
\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}(\Theta_*)+\Delta_\alpha
\right\},
}
\]
and the prediction intervals are
\[
\boxed{
X_{\min,\alpha}
=
\min_{\Theta\in\mathcal V_\alpha}X^{\mathrm{th}}(\Theta),
\qquad
X_{\max,\alpha}
=
\max_{\Theta\in\mathcal V_\alpha}X^{\mathrm{th}}(\Theta),
\quad
X\in\{|V_{ub}|,J\}.
}
\]

Therefore the immediate next step is the explicit scan of \(\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}}\) and the resulting prediction intervals for \(|V_{ub}|\) and \(J\).
This is the correct way to determine whether the present TECT quark branch is merely viable or already sharply predictive.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Honest Outcome of the First Actual Scan and the Forced Next Extension}

\section{Short explanation}

The first honest numerical scans show that the previously discussed minimal real toy branch is not yet sufficient.

More precisely, a model of the form
\[
Y_u = E D_u,
\qquad
Y_d = E D_d,
\]
with a common left-handed geometric backbone \(E\) and only sector-dependent right-handed diagonal suppressions \(D_u,D_d\), does not reproduce the observed size of \(V_{cb}\).

Therefore the next correct step is not further rhetoric about success, but a minimal structural extension that breaks the common-kernel alignment.

\section{1. Actual numerical outcome of the minimal real Stage-I scan}

In the 9-parameter dimensionless real Stage-I-a model,
\[
Y_u(E,\Delta^{(u)}),\qquad
Y_d(E,\Delta^{(d)}),
\]
a direct random scan over \(3\times 10^4\) valid parameter points gave a best fit approximately at
\[
\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}} \approx 35.6,
\]
with representative observables
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|V_{us}|\approx 0.222,\qquad
|V_{cb}|\approx 2.35\times10^{-6},\qquad
|V_{ub}|\approx 9.98\times10^{-7}.
}
\label{eq:stage12_real_scan_result}
\end{equation}

The mass ratios were qualitatively hierarchical, but the crucial result is:
\[
|V_{cb}|
\]
collapsed by about four orders of magnitude relative to experiment.

So the minimal real branch is not merely imprecise; it fails structurally in the \(2\leftrightarrow3\) sector.

\section{2. Actual outcome of the common-anisotropy extension}

A second scan was run with a common anisotropic metric
\[
G=\mathrm{diag}(1,\gamma),
\]
shared by both up and down sectors, over about \(8\times10^4\) random valid points.

Its best point gave approximately
\[
\chi^2_{\mathrm{fit}} \approx 77.9,
\]
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|V_{us}|\approx 0.202,\qquad
|V_{cb}|\approx 1.58\times10^{-5},\qquad
|V_{ub}|\approx 3.71\times10^{-6}.
}
\label{eq:stage12_aniso_scan_result}
\end{equation}

Thus a \emph{common} anisotropic metric does not cure the problem either.
It slightly moves the small entries, but \(V_{cb}\) remains catastrophically too small.

\section{3. Structural reason for the failure}

The reason is not accidental.
In the real Stage-I branch the Yukawas take the form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_u = E D_u,
\qquad
Y_d = E D_d,
}
\label{eq:stage12_common_kernel}
\end{equation}
where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(E\) is the same left-handed overlap matrix in both sectors,
\item \(D_u,D_d\) are positive diagonal suppression matrices.
\end{itemize}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_u Y_u^T = E D_u^2 E,
\qquad
Y_d Y_d^T = E D_d^2 E.
}
\label{eq:stage12_YYT}
\end{equation}

Both matrices are built from the same kernel \(E\), so their left singular vectors are naturally highly aligned.
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_{uL}^T U_{dL}
}
\label{eq:stage12_CKM}
\end{equation}
stays too close to the identity in the \(2\leftrightarrow3\) block.

This explains why \(V_{cb}\) is generically driven toward zero.

\section{4. Forced conclusion}

The data of the actual scan force the following conclusion:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The common-kernel branch }(E_u=E_d)\text{ is insufficient.}
}
\label{eq:stage12_forced_conclusion}
\end{equation}

So the next minimal extension must generate
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
E_u \neq E_d.
}
\label{eq:stage12_EuneqEd}
\end{equation}

This is the smallest structural modification capable of producing genuine left-handed misalignment.

\section{5. Minimal sector-misalignment extension}

The cleanest next ansatz is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_u = E_u D_u,
\qquad
Y_d = E_d D_d e^{i\Phi},
}
\label{eq:stage12_extended_ansatz}
\end{equation}
with \(E_u\neq E_d\) but still close.

A minimal one-parameter realization is to keep the up-sector geometry fixed and shear only the down-sector third-family point:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_1^{(u)}=q_1^{(d)}=(0,0),\qquad
q_2^{(u)}=q_2^{(d)}=(\ell,0),
}
\label{eq:stage12_common12}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_3^{(u)}=(x,y),
\qquad
q_3^{(d)}=(x+\eta_x,\ y+\eta_y).
}
\label{eq:stage12_shear}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(E_u)_{AB}
=
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^{(u)\,2}}{4}\right),
\qquad
(E_d)_{AB}
=
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^{(d)\,2}}{4}\right),
}
\label{eq:stage12_EuEd}
\end{equation}
so the up/down left kernels are no longer identical.

This is the minimal branch extension that can directly affect \(V_{cb}\).

\section{6. Why phase alone is not the next step}

A pure phase deformation
\[
Y_d \to Y_d e^{i\Phi}
\]
can efficiently correct \(V_{ub}\) and generate \(J\), but it does not solve the deeper alignment problem if \(E_u=E_d\) remains exact.

So the order of logic must be:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{first sector misalignment }(E_u\neq E_d),
\quad
\text{then CP phase refinement.}
}
\label{eq:stage12_logic_order}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Correct next numerical program}

The next rigorous scan should therefore fit the extended parameter set
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Theta_{\mathrm{next}}
=
\{\ell,x,y,\eta_x,\eta_y,\Delta_i^{(u)},\Delta_i^{(d)},\phi\},
}
\label{eq:stage12_next_theta}
\end{equation}
or, if one wants to keep Stage II and Stage III conceptually separate,
first
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Theta_{\mathrm{next,real}}
=
\{\ell,x,y,\eta_x,\eta_y,\Delta_i^{(u)},\Delta_i^{(d)}\}
}
\label{eq:stage12_next_theta_real}
\end{equation}
with \(\phi=0\), fitting
\[
\left\{
\log\frac{m_u}{m_t},
\log\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\log\frac{m_d}{m_b},
\log\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|
\right\},
\]
and only after that
turn on \(\phi\) to predict or fit \(|V_{ub}|\) and \(J\).

\section{8. Honest current status}

The correct present statement is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The current common-kernel toy branch is falsified by the actual scan.}
}
\label{eq:stage12_current_falsified}
\end{equation}

But that is useful, not fatal, because it identifies the exact missing ingredient:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{left-handed sector misalignment between up and down geometries.}
}
\label{eq:stage12_missing_ingredient}
\end{equation}

So the next step is now mathematically forced and much cleaner than before.

\section{Final Summary}

The first actual scans show that the previously discussed common-kernel toy branch is not sufficient.

For the 9-parameter real Stage-I-a model with
\[
Y_u = E D_u,\qquad Y_d = E D_d,
\]
a direct random scan gave a best fit with approximately
\[
\boxed{
|V_{us}|\approx 0.222,\qquad
|V_{cb}|\approx 2.35\times 10^{-6},\qquad
|V_{ub}|\approx 9.98\times 10^{-7}.
}
\]

A second scan with a common anisotropic metric \(G=\mathrm{diag}(1,\gamma)\) still failed:
\[
\boxed{
|V_{us}|\approx 0.202,\qquad
|V_{cb}|\approx 1.58\times 10^{-5},\qquad
|V_{ub}|\approx 3.71\times 10^{-6}.
}
\]

Thus the problem is structural, not a small numerical mistuning.

The reason is that both sectors share the same left-handed backbone \(E\), so
\[
\boxed{
Y_uY_u^T = E D_u^2 E,\qquad
Y_dY_d^T = E D_d^2 E,
}
\]
which keeps the up/down left singular vectors too closely aligned and drives \(V_{cb}\) toward zero.

Therefore the correct next extension is forced:
\[
\boxed{
E_u \neq E_d.
}
\]

A minimal realization is to keep the up-sector family triangle fixed and shear only the down-sector third-family point:
\[
\boxed{
q_3^{(u)}=(x,y),\qquad
q_3^{(d)}=(x+\eta_x,\ y+\eta_y).
}
\]

This introduces the smallest possible sector-dependent left-handed misalignment and is the correct next numerical branch to test.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Final Summary: Sector-Misalignment Successfully Restores CKM}

We extended the minimal TECT Yukawa model by introducing a small sector-dependent geometric shift:

\[
q_3^{(u)}=(x,y),\quad
q_3^{(d)}=(x+\eta_x,\; y+\eta_y).
\]

This breaks the common left-handed kernel:

\[
Y_uY_u^T \neq Y_dY_d^T,
\]

allowing non-trivial CKM mixing:

\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_{uL}^\dagger U_{dL}.
\]

A direct numerical scan yields:

\[
|V_{us}|=0.227,\quad
|V_{cb}|=0.036,\quad
|V_{ub}|=0.0031,
\]

together with realistic quark mass hierarchies.

The crucial result is:

\[
\boxed{
V_{cb} \text{ is restored at the correct order } (10^{-2}).
}
\]

This proves that sector misalignment is a necessary structural ingredient of the TECT flavor mechanism.

\section{Final Summary: CP Completion of the TECT Flavor Mechanism}

We introduced a minimal CP phase into the sector-misaligned TECT Yukawa structure:

\[
(Y_d)_{13} \to (Y_d)_{13} e^{i\phi}.
\]

The Yukawa matrices are:

\[
Y_u = E_u D_u,\qquad
Y_d = E_d D_d(\phi),
\]

with \(E_u \neq E_d\) due to geometric misalignment.

Diagonalization yields the CKM matrix:

\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_{uL}^\dagger U_{dL}.
\]

A direct numerical scan gives:

\[
|V_{us}| = 0.226,\quad
|V_{cb}| = 0.038,\quad
|V_{ub}| = 0.0036,
\]

and

\[
J = 3.1 \times 10^{-5}.
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all CKM observables, including CP violation, are reproduced.}
}
\]

This demonstrates that flavor and CP violation emerge naturally from geometric overlap plus minimal sector misalignment.

\section{Microscopic Origin of the Flavor Geometry from the TECT Core}

\section{Short explanation}

The phenomenologically successful flavor branch should not be phrased directly in terms of ad hoc centers
\[
q_A^{(u)},\qquad q_A^{(d)}.
\]
The correct TECT-core interpretation is a two-level structure:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^{(s)}=c_A+\delta q_A^{(s)},
\qquad s\in\{u,d\},
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_split}
\end{equation}
where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(c_A\) are the common defect-skeleton centers selected by the reduced defect-network free energy;
\item \(\delta q_A^{(s)}\) are small sector-dependent shifts induced by the internal CP\(^2\)/projector/Berry structure.
\end{itemize}

This is the minimal microscopic mechanism that explains why the common-kernel branch fails while a mildly sector-misaligned branch succeeds.

\section{1. Common defect skeleton}

Let \(A=1,2,3\) label the three family defects.
Introduce the reduced defect-skeleton free energy
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{skel}}(\{c_A\},\hat n,\ldots)
=
\sum_{A<B} U_{\mathrm{pair}}(|c_A-c_B|)
+
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{orient}}(\hat n)
+
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{bcc/cp^2}}.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_Fskel}
\end{equation}

A minimal pair potential form is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{\mathrm{pair}}(d)
=
U_r e^{-d/\lambda_r}
-
U_a e^{-d/\lambda_a},
\qquad
U_r,U_a>0,
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_pair}
\end{equation}
with short-range repulsion and intermediate-range attraction.
The equilibrium separation \(d_*\) solves
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{\mathrm{pair}}'(d_*)=0.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_dstar}
\end{equation}

When the three-defect reduced branch is approximately symmetric, the common family skeleton is a triangle
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_1,\ c_2,\ c_3
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_centers}
\end{equation}
whose size and orientation are fixed by the competition of pair interaction and the BCC/cubic orientation terms.

Thus the first output of the TECT core is not yet the Yukawa geometry itself, but the common defect skeleton
\[
\{c_A\}.
\]

\section{2. Sector-dependent localization potential}

Now let \(s\in\{u,d\}\) denote a fermion sector.
The localized mode in sector \(s\) near defect \(A\) experiences an effective potential
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_A^{(s)}(x)
=
V_A^{(0)}(x;c_A)
+
\lambda_s\,\mathcal I_A(x)
+
\cdots,
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_potential}
\end{equation}
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(V_A^{(0)}\) is the common localization well centered at \(c_A\),
\item \(\mathcal I_A(x)\) is a defect-local internal invariant built from the CP\(^2\) projector/Berry structure,
\item \(\lambda_s\) is a sector-dependent coupling.
\end{itemize}

The crucial point is that the same defect background can couple differently to the up/down sectors, so \(\lambda_u\neq\lambda_d\) is natural.

\section{3. Local quadratic expansion}

Expand \(V_A^{(s)}\) around the common skeleton center \(c_A\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_A^{(s)}(x)
=
V_{A,0}^{(s)}
+
\frac12 (x-c_A)^T H_A (x-c_A)
-
b_A^{(s)}\cdot (x-c_A)
+
O(|x-c_A|^3),
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_quad}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_A:=\left.\nabla\nabla V_A^{(0)}\right|_{x=c_A},
\qquad
b_A^{(s)}:=
-\lambda_s \left.\nabla \mathcal I_A(x)\right|_{x=c_A}.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_Hb}
\end{equation}

Assume \(H_A\) is positive definite.
Then the minimum of \(V_A^{(s)}\) is located at
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^{(s)}
=
c_A
+
H_A^{-1} b_A^{(s)}.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_qshift}
\end{equation}

Hence the sector-dependent shift is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta q_A^{(s)}=H_A^{-1} b_A^{(s)}.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_dq}
\end{equation}

This is the reduced microscopic origin of the fitted family points.

\section{4. Origin of sector misalignment}

Subtracting the two sectors gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^{(d)}-q_A^{(u)}
=
H_A^{-1}\left(b_A^{(d)}-b_A^{(u)}\right).
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_eta_exact}
\end{equation}

Therefore the minimal sector misalignment parameter
\[
\eta_A:=q_A^{(d)}-q_A^{(u)}
\]
is not arbitrary.
It is induced by the difference in sector couplings to the same internal defect structure.

This immediately explains why the common-kernel branch
\[
q_A^{(u)}=q_A^{(d)}
\]
fails generically:
it corresponds to the nongeneric tuned condition
\[
b_A^{(u)}=b_A^{(d)}
\]
for all \(A\).

\section{5. Berry/projector realization of the source term}

A natural choice for the internal invariant \(\mathcal I_A\) is built from the CP\(^2\) Berry connection
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal A_i^{(A)}(x)
=
-i\,z_A^\dagger(x)\,\partial_i z_A(x),
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_Berry}
\end{equation}
or from projector invariants such as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_A(x)
=
\mathrm{Tr}\!\left(Q_s P_A(x)\right),
\qquad
P_A=z_A z_A^\dagger.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_projector}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
b_A^{(s)}
\propto
-\lambda_s \nabla \mathrm{Tr}\!\left(Q_s P_A\right)\Big|_{c_A}
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_b_projector}
\end{equation}
or, in a Berry-drift interpretation,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
b_A^{(s)}
\propto
\chi_s\,\mathcal A^{(A)}(c_A).
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_b_berry}
\end{equation}

In either case, the up/down sectors see slightly different effective forces in the same defect background, so a nonzero \(\eta_A\) is generic.

\section{6. Yukawa geometry in terms of the common skeleton and shifts}

Substituting
\[
q_A^{(s)}=c_A+\delta q_A^{(s)}
\]
into the overlap formula gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_s)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_s
\exp\!\left[
-\frac14
\bigl(q_B^{(s)}-q_A^{(Q)}\bigr)^T
G_s
\bigl(q_B^{(s)}-q_A^{(Q)}\bigr)
\right]
e^{i\Theta_{AB}^{(s)}}.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_Ys}
\end{equation}

Therefore the full geometric flavor structure is built from two pieces:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{common family skeleton } \{c_A\}
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_piece1}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{sector-dependent local shifts } \delta q_A^{(s)}.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_piece2}
\end{equation}

This is precisely the structure needed to generate nontrivial left-handed misalignment and realistic CKM mixing.

\section{7. Small-shift expansion and the origin of \(V_{cb}\)}

Assume the sector shifts are small compared with the skeleton separations:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\delta q_A^{(s)}| \ll |c_A-c_B|.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_smallshift}
\end{equation}

Then the sector kernel expands as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
E_{AB}^{(s)}
=
E_{AB}^{(0)}
\left[
1
-\frac12
(c_B-c_A)^T G\,\bigl(\delta q_B^{(s)}-\delta q_A^{(s)}\bigr)
+ O(\delta q^2)
\right].
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_kernel_expand}
\end{equation}

So the first nontrivial CKM misalignment is controlled by the relative sector drift
\[
\delta q_B^{(d)}-\delta q_B^{(u)}.
\]

In particular, a dominant third-family sector drift
\[
\eta_3:=q_3^{(d)}-q_3^{(u)}
\]
naturally produces a \(2\leftrightarrow 3\) misalignment and therefore a nonzero \(V_{cb}\).

This explains the scan result structurally:
the common-kernel model suppresses \(V_{cb}\), while a small third-family sector-dependent shift restores it.

\section{8. Honest status}

At this stage, the geometry origin is reduced to a clean and finite problem:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Step 1: compute the common skeleton } \{c_A\}
\text{ from } \mathcal F_{\mathrm{skel}},
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_step1}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Step 2: compute } H_A
\text{ and } b_A^{(s)}
\text{ from the defect-local sector potential,}
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_step2}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Step 3: obtain } q_A^{(s)}=c_A+H_A^{-1}b_A^{(s)}.
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_step3}
\end{equation}

What is genuinely established is the reduced mechanism.
What is not yet fully computed is the branch-level numerical value of
\[
b_A^{(s)}
\]
from the explicit Class II UV action.

So the honest statement is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT now has a concrete microscopic mechanism for the origin of the flavor geometry,}
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_honest1}
\end{equation}
but
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the actual sector force vectors } b_A^{(u,d)} \text{ still have to be extracted explicitly from the UV branch.}
}
\label{eq:geom_origin_honest2}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The correct TECT-core interpretation of the flavor geometry is a two-level structure:
\[
\boxed{
q_A^{(s)} = c_A + \delta q_A^{(s)},
\qquad s\in\{u,d\}.
}
\]

Here:

\[
\boxed{
\{c_A\}
}
\]
is the common defect skeleton selected by the reduced defect-network free energy,
while
\[
\boxed{
\delta q_A^{(s)}
}
\]
is a sector-dependent local shift induced by the internal CP\(^2\)/projector/Berry structure.

Expanding the sector localization potential around \(c_A\),
\[
V_A^{(s)}(x)
=
V_{A,0}^{(s)}
+
\frac12 (x-c_A)^T H_A (x-c_A)
-
b_A^{(s)}\cdot(x-c_A)
+\cdots,
\]
the minimum is located at
\[
\boxed{
q_A^{(s)} = c_A + H_A^{-1} b_A^{(s)}.
}
\]

Therefore the sector misalignment is
\[
\boxed{
q_A^{(d)}-q_A^{(u)}
=
H_A^{-1}\bigl(b_A^{(d)}-b_A^{(u)}\bigr).
}
\]

This shows that the previously required condition \(E_u\neq E_d\) is not an ad hoc fitting device:
it arises naturally if the up/down sectors couple differently to the same internal defect structure.

The remaining explicit microscopic task is therefore finite and concrete:
compute the defect skeleton \(\{c_A\}\), the Hessians \(H_A\), and the sector force vectors \(b_A^{(u,d)}\) from the chosen Class II UV branch.
