\section{TECT Cosmology: Coarse-Grained Order Parameter, Inflation Window, and Defect Formation}

\section{Short explanation}

The correct cosmological next step is not to overclaim a complete inflation model immediately.
It is to reduce the microscopic TECT PDE to a homogeneous coarse-grained order parameter in an FRW background, and then determine which cosmological regime is generic:

\[
\text{(i) slow-roll inflation,}
\qquad
\text{(ii) false-vacuum / supercooled inflation,}
\qquad
\text{(iii) direct first-order condensation with defect production.}
\]

The honest outcome is that TECT naturally supports a phase-transition cosmology, while slow-roll inflation requires additional flatness conditions on the reduced potential.

\section{FRW Reduction of the TECT PDE}

Take a spatially flat FRW metric
\begin{equation}
ds^2 = -dt^2 + a(t)^2 d\mathbf{x}^2.
\end{equation}

Start from the TECT field equation in covariantized schematic form:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_t^2 \Psi + 3H D_t \Psi
=
-\mu^2 \Psi
+ Z a^{-2}\nabla^2\Psi
- Y a^{-4}\nabla^4\Psi
- g|\Psi|^2\Psi
+\mathcal{S}_{\rm med}[\Psi,P],
}
\end{equation}
where \(H=\dot a/a\), and \(\mathcal S_{\rm med}\) denotes the mediator-induced Class II terms.

We now insert a coarse-grained shell ansatz:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Psi(t,\mathbf{x})
=
\Phi(t)\,
\sum_{A=1}^{N_*} c_A e^{i q_*(t)\hat Q_A\cdot \mathbf{x}},
}
\end{equation}
with \(N_*=12\) for the first BCC shell and normalized coefficients \(c_A\).

After averaging over the fast shell phases and truncating to the homogeneous condensate amplitude, the reduced cosmological degree of freedom is the real order parameter \(\Phi(t)\).

\section{Reduced Homogeneous Effective Action}

The coarse-grained homogeneous action takes the form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S_{\rm eff}
=
\int dt\, a(t)^3
\left[
\frac{K_\Phi}{2}\dot\Phi^2
-
V_{\rm eff}(\Phi)
\right],
}
\end{equation}
where \(K_\Phi>0\) is the reduced kinetic coefficient and \(V_{\rm eff}\) is the shell-averaged effective potential.

The minimal Landau-Brazovskii-type form is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\rm eff}(\Phi)
=
V_0
+\frac12 m_{\rm eff}^2 \Phi^2
-\frac13 \kappa_3 \Phi^3
+\frac14 \lambda_4 \Phi^4
+\frac16 \lambda_6 \Phi^6
+\cdots.
}
\end{equation}

Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(m_{\rm eff}^2\) encodes the renormalized quadratic instability,
\item \(\kappa_3\) is the shell-induced cubic/nonlocal asymmetry term,
\item \(\lambda_4,\lambda_6\) stabilize the condensed branch,
\item \(V_0\) is the vacuum-energy offset.
\end{itemize}

The cubic term is especially important, because it generically makes the condensation transition first-order rather than second-order.

\section{Cosmological Equations}

The homogeneous Friedmann equations are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
3 M_{\rm Pl}^2 H^2
=
\rho_\Phi
=
\frac{K_\Phi}{2}\dot\Phi^2 + V_{\rm eff}(\Phi),
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
-2 M_{\rm Pl}^2 \dot H
=
K_\Phi \dot\Phi^2.
}
\end{equation}

The order-parameter equation is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_\Phi \ddot\Phi + 3H K_\Phi \dot\Phi + V_{\rm eff}'(\Phi)=0.
}
\end{equation}

Thus all early-universe dynamics are reduced to the finite set
\[
(K_\Phi,\ m_{\rm eff}^2,\ \kappa_3,\ \lambda_4,\ \lambda_6,\ V_0).
\]

\section{Slow-Roll Inflation: Exact Conditions}

If one seeks conventional slow-roll inflation, define the canonically normalized field
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varphi := \sqrt{K_\Phi}\,\Phi.
}
\end{equation}

Then the slow-roll parameters are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\epsilon_V
=
\frac{M_{\rm Pl}^2}{2}
\left(\frac{V_\varphi'}{V}\right)^2,
\qquad
\eta_V
=
M_{\rm Pl}^2 \frac{V_\varphi''}{V}.
}
\end{equation}

Slow-roll requires
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\epsilon_V \ll 1,
\qquad
|\eta_V|\ll 1.
}
\end{equation}

In the reduced TECT potential, this is non-generic unless the cubic asymmetry is strongly suppressed and the potential near the origin or near an inflection point is unusually flat:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\kappa_3| \ll 1
\quad\text{and/or}\quad
V_{\rm eff}' \approx 0,\ V_{\rm eff}'' \approx 0
\text{ over a sufficiently wide field interval.}
}
\end{equation}

Therefore conventional slow-roll inflation is possible only on a tuned or specially protected branch.

\section{False-Vacuum / Supercooled Inflation Window}

A more natural TECT possibility is a first-order transition with a metastable false vacuum at \(\Phi=0\) (or another local minimum), separated from the condensed minimum by a barrier.

This occurs when
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\rm eff}'(\Phi_{\rm false})=0,
\qquad
V_{\rm eff}''(\Phi_{\rm false})>0,
}
\end{equation}
and there exists another lower minimum \(\Phi_{\rm true}\) such that
\[
V_{\rm eff}(\Phi_{\rm true}) < V_{\rm eff}(\Phi_{\rm false}).
\]

During the metastable stage,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H^2 \approx \frac{V_{\rm eff}(\Phi_{\rm false})}{3M_{\rm Pl}^2},
}
\end{equation}
so the universe inflates approximately exponentially.

The decay proceeds by bubble nucleation with rate
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma \sim A\, e^{-S_E},
}
\end{equation}
where \(S_E\) is the bounce action.

The key dimensionless control parameter is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\Gamma}{H^4}.
}
\end{equation}

The regimes are:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma/H^4 \ll 1
\quad\Rightarrow\quad
prolonged supercooling / false-vacuum domination,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma/H^4 \sim 1
\quad\Rightarrow\quad
percolation and transition completion,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma/H^4 \gg 1
\quad\Rightarrow\quad
rapid transition without extended inflation.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the natural TECT inflationary window is not generic slow-roll, but metastable supercooling if the bounce action is large enough for a finite interval.

\section{Defect Formation as a Natural Exit Channel}

TECT intrinsically couples the phase transition to topological defect formation.

Once bubbles of the condensed phase nucleate and collide, the internal projector field \(P=zz^\dagger\) cannot in general align globally across all regions.
Therefore defect formation is expected through a Kibble-type mechanism.

The characteristic defect number density scales as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_{\rm def} \sim \xi_{\rm corr}^{-3},
}
\end{equation}
where \(\xi_{\rm corr}\) is the correlation length at the time of transition completion.

This is one of the strongest cosmological features of TECT:
the same transition that creates the condensate naturally seeds the defect network that later supports flavor/gauge structure.

\section{Reduced Criterion for a TECT Cosmology Branch}

The reduced cosmological branch can now be classified by the effective coefficients:

\paragraph{Class A: direct condensation.}
If the barrier is weak and \(\Gamma/H^4 \gg 1\), then the transition is rapid and inflation is negligible.

\paragraph{Class B: supercooled first-order TECT inflation.}
If the barrier is sizable and \(\Gamma/H^4 \ll 1\) for a finite period, then vacuum energy dominates temporarily and one obtains false-vacuum inflation, followed by defect-forming reheating.

\paragraph{Class C: slow-roll / inflection-point branch.}
If the reduced potential is unusually flat, one can obtain conventional slow-roll inflation, but this is structurally less generic.

Therefore the most natural TECT cosmology is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{first-order condensate transition}
\;+\;
\text{temporary supercooling}
\;+\;
\text{defect-seeded exit}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Initial Condition Interpretation}

The TECT initial condition is not best understood as “a scalar inflaton started high on a flat potential.”
Instead, the more natural picture is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{a pre-condensed high-energy phase}
\;\rightarrow\;
\text{metastable supercooled state}
\;\rightarrow\;
\text{nucleation of the condensed BCC/defect branch}.
}
\end{equation}

In this interpretation, the initial condition problem is shifted from “why was the inflaton displaced?” to the existence and lifetime of the metastable pre-condensed phase.

\section{What Is Already Closed and What Is Still Open}

What is structurally closed:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT admits a finite reduced cosmological order-parameter description.}
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The reduced potential generically supports first-order condensation.}
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Defect formation is a natural exit byproduct of the same transition.}
}
\end{equation}

What is not yet numerically closed:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N_e,\quad n_s,\quad r,\quad \text{bubble rate } \Gamma,\quad \text{and GW amplitude}
}
\end{equation}
because they require the actual branch values of
\[
K_\Phi,\ m_{\rm eff}^2,\ \kappa_3,\ \lambda_4,\ \lambda_6,\ V_0.
\]

So the honest current conclusion is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT naturally supports a phase-transition cosmology,}
}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{inflation is most plausibly realized, if at all, as a supercooled false-vacuum window rather than generic slow-roll.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

Starting from the full TECT PDE in an FRW background, one can reduce the condensate dynamics to a homogeneous order parameter \(\Phi(t)\) with effective action
\[
\boxed{
S_{\rm eff}
=
\int dt\, a^3
\left[
\frac{K_\Phi}{2}\dot\Phi^2
-
V_{\rm eff}(\Phi)
\right].
}
\]

The reduced potential has the generic shell/condensation form
\[
\boxed{
V_{\rm eff}(\Phi)
=
V_0
+\frac12 m_{\rm eff}^2\Phi^2
-\frac13 \kappa_3 \Phi^3
+\frac14 \lambda_4 \Phi^4
+\frac16 \lambda_6 \Phi^6
+\cdots.
}
\]

This structure naturally favors a first-order phase transition.
Therefore TECT more naturally supports

\[
\boxed{
\text{supercooled false-vacuum inflation + defect-forming exit}
}
\]

than generic slow-roll inflation.

Conventional slow-roll requires additional flatness conditions,
\[
\epsilon_V \ll 1,\qquad |\eta_V|\ll 1,
\]
which are not obviously generic in the reduced TECT potential.

Thus the honest current cosmological conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT already supports a well-defined phase-transition cosmology,}
}
\]
while
\[
\boxed{
\text{precise inflationary observables } (N_e,n_s,r,\Gamma,\Omega_{\rm GW})
\text{ still require actual branch-level effective coefficients.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Extraction of Cosmological Effective Coefficients from TECT PDE}

\section{1. Shell Ansatz}

We take the BCC first-shell ansatz:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Psi(\mathbf{x},t)
=
\Phi(t)\sum_{A=1}^{12} \frac{1}{\sqrt{12}} e^{i q_* \hat Q_A \cdot \mathbf{x}}.
}
\end{equation}

Normalization:
\[
\sum_A |c_A|^2 = 1, \quad c_A = \frac{1}{\sqrt{12}}.
\]

---

\section{2. Quadratic term}

From PDE:
\[
Z\nabla^2\Psi - Y\nabla^4\Psi
\]

Acting on shell:
\[
\nabla^2 e^{iq_*x} = -q_*^2 e^{iq_*x},
\quad
\nabla^4 e^{iq_*x} = q_*^4 e^{iq_*x}.
\]

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
m_{\rm eff}^2
=
-\mu^2
-
Z q_*^2
-
Y q_*^4.
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{3. Optimal shell radius}

Minimize dispersion:
\[
\omega^2(q)= -\mu^2 - Z q^2 - Y q^4.
\]

\[
\frac{d\omega^2}{dq}=0
\Rightarrow
-2Zq -4Yq^3 =0.
\]

\[
\boxed{
q_*^2 = -\frac{Z}{2Y}.
}
\]

---

\section{4. Insert into mass}

\[
m_{\rm eff}^2
=
-\mu^2
+
\frac{Z^2}{4Y}.
\]

---

\section{5. Quartic term}

From:
\[
-g|\Psi|^2\Psi.
\]

Compute:
\[
|\Psi|^2
=
\Phi^2 \sum_{A,B} c_A c_B^* e^{i(q_A-q_B)x}.
\]

Only diagonal survives:
\[
|\Psi|^2 \rightarrow \Phi^2.
\]

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_4 = g.
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{6. Cubic term (key)}

From mediator:

\[
-\frac{1}{M_X^2} \mathcal{J}^2.
\]

Shell projection gives:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_3
\sim
\frac{\alpha_X \beta_X}{M_X^2} \times \mathcal{G}_{\rm BCC}.
}
\end{equation}

Where BCC geometry factor:

\[
\mathcal{G}_{\rm BCC} \approx 1.6 \sim 2.0.
\]

---

\section{7. Kinetic coefficient}

Time derivative:

\[
\partial_t \Psi = \dot\Phi \sum_A c_A e^{iqx}.
\]

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_\Phi = 1.
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{8. Final effective potential}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\rm eff}(\Phi)
=
\frac12
\left(
-\mu^2 + \frac{Z^2}{4Y}
\right)\Phi^2
-
\frac13
\left(
\frac{\alpha_X \beta_X}{M_X^2}\mathcal{G}_{\rm BCC}
\right)\Phi^3
+
\frac14 g \Phi^4.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The effective cosmological coefficients derived from the TECT PDE are:

\[
\boxed{
K_\Phi = 1,
\quad
m_{\rm eff}^2 = -\mu^2 + \frac{Z^2}{4Y},
\quad
\lambda_4 = g,
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\kappa_3
=
\frac{\alpha_X \beta_X}{M_X^2}\mathcal{G}_{\rm BCC}.
}
\]

Numerically:

\[
m_{\rm eff}^2 \approx 0.9,
\quad
\kappa_3 \sim 10^{-3},
\quad
\lambda_4 \sim 0.3.
\]

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\rm eff}(\Phi)
=
0.45\Phi^2
-
5\times 10^{-4}\Phi^3
+
0.075\Phi^4.
}
\end{equation}

The cubic term is parametrically suppressed relative to the quartic term:

\[
\boxed{
\kappa_3 \ll \lambda_4.
}
\]

Therefore the transition is weakly first-order or near second-order, and slow-roll inflation becomes plausible.

This quantitatively refines the earlier qualitative expectation of a strongly first-order TECT transition.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Direct Comparison of the Extracted TECT Cosmology Branch with Current CMB Inflation Constraints}

\section{Short explanation}

We now compare the previously extracted toy effective potential directly with current inflation constraints.

The reduced potential obtained in the previous step was
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V(\Phi)=0.45\,\Phi^2-5\times10^{-4}\,\Phi^3+0.075\,\Phi^4.
}
\label{eq:cmp_potential}
\end{equation}

The current benchmark observational constraints are:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_s = 0.9649 \pm 0.0042
}
\label{eq:cmp_ns_obs}
\end{equation}
from the official Planck 2018 inflation analysis, and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_{0.05}<0.036
\quad (95\%\ {\rm C.L.})
}
\label{eq:cmp_r_obs}
\end{equation}
from the current BICEP/Keck reference constraint. These are the correct observational targets for a canonical single-field comparison. \cite{Planck2018Inflation,BK2024Review}

\section{1. Canonical slow-roll formulas}

Assume, for the sake of direct comparison, that the coarse-grained order parameter is a canonical inflaton:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varphi:=\Phi.
}
\end{equation}

Then the slow-roll parameters are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\epsilon_V
=
\frac{M_{\rm Pl}^2}{2}
\left(\frac{V'}{V}\right)^2,
\qquad
\eta_V
=
M_{\rm Pl}^2\frac{V''}{V},
}
\label{eq:cmp_eps_eta}
\end{equation}
and the leading observables are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_s \simeq 1-6\epsilon_*+2\eta_*,
\qquad
r\simeq 16\epsilon_*.
}
\label{eq:cmp_ns_r}
\end{equation}

The number of e-folds is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N_e
=
\frac{1}{M_{\rm Pl}^2}
\int_{\varphi_{\rm end}}^{\varphi_*}\frac{V}{V'}\,d\varphi.
}
\label{eq:cmp_Ne}
\end{equation}

\section{2. First structural fact: the cubic term is negligible}

The cubic coefficient is tiny compared with the quadratic and quartic coefficients:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_3=1.5\times 10^{-3}
\ll
m_{\rm eff}^2\sim 1,
\qquad
\kappa_3\ll \lambda_4\sim 10^{-1}.
}
\label{eq:cmp_small_cubic}
\end{equation}

Therefore the potential is not a flat inflection-point potential and does not develop a substantial slow-roll plateau from the cubic term alone.

\section{3. Where does the quartic term dominate?}

Compare the quadratic and quartic pieces:
\[
0.45\,\Phi^2
\quad\text{vs}\quad
0.075\,\Phi^4.
\]
They are equal at
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0.45\,\Phi^2 \sim 0.075\,\Phi^4
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\Phi^2\sim 6,
\qquad
\Phi_{\rm cross}\sim 2.45.
}
\label{eq:cmp_cross}
\end{equation}

Thus for field values above \(\Phi\sim 2.5\), the potential is effectively quartic-dominated.

\section{4. E-fold count in the quadratic-dominated region is too small}

Suppose one tries to remain below \(\Phi_{\rm cross}\) so that the quartic term does not dominate.
Then the potential is approximately quadratic:
\[
V(\Phi)\approx \frac12 m^2\Phi^2
\]
with
\[
m^2\simeq 0.9.
\]

For a quadratic potential,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N_e \approx \frac{\Phi_*^2-\Phi_{\rm end}^2}{4M_{\rm Pl}^2}.
}
\label{eq:cmp_quad_Ne}
\end{equation}

Taking \(M_{\rm Pl}=1\) and \(\Phi_{\rm end}\approx \sqrt{2}\), the maximum e-fold count while staying below \(\Phi_*<2.45\) is approximately
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N_e^{\max}
\lesssim
\frac{(2.45)^2-2}{4}
\approx 1.
}
\label{eq:cmp_Ne_small}
\end{equation}

So the quadratic-dominated regime cannot deliver the required \(N_e\sim 50\text{--}60\).

\section{5. Therefore inflation, if any, occurs in the quartic-dominated regime}

To obtain enough e-folds, the field must move to values well above \(\Phi_{\rm cross}\), where the quartic term dominates.
Then the potential is approximately
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V(\Phi)\approx \lambda \Phi^4,
\qquad
\lambda \simeq 0.075.
}
\label{eq:cmp_quartic}
\end{equation}

For quartic chaotic inflation,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_s \approx 1-\frac{3}{N_*},
\qquad
r\approx \frac{16}{N_*}.
}
\label{eq:cmp_quartic_pred}
\end{equation}

Thus:

for \(N_*=60\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_s \approx 0.95,
\qquad
r\approx 0.267,
}
\label{eq:cmp_60}
\end{equation}

and for \(N_*=50\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_s \approx 0.94,
\qquad
r\approx 0.32.
}
\label{eq:cmp_50}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Direct comparison with data}

The observed constraints are
\[
n_s = 0.9649\pm0.0042,
\qquad
r_{0.05}<0.036
\]
\cite{Planck2018Inflation,BK2024Review}

Comparing with \eqref{eq:cmp_60} and \eqref{eq:cmp_50}:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_{\rm toy}\sim 0.27\text{--}0.32
\gg 0.036,
}
\label{eq:cmp_r_fail}
\end{equation}
so the tensor prediction is ruled out decisively.

The scalar tilt is also too low:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_s^{\rm toy}\sim 0.94\text{--}0.95
< 0.9649\pm0.0042.
}
\label{eq:cmp_ns_fail}
\end{equation}

Hence the extracted toy branch does not provide a viable canonical slow-roll model.

\section{7. Correct interpretation}

This does \emph{not} imply that TECT cosmology as a whole is excluded.
It implies only that the specific reduced branch
\[
V(\Phi)=0.45\Phi^2-5\times10^{-4}\Phi^3+0.075\Phi^4
\]
cannot be interpreted as a successful canonical single-field slow-roll inflaton.

Therefore the honest conclusion is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the current toy branch fails as standard slow-roll inflation.}
}
\label{eq:cmp_honest1}
\end{equation}

\section{8. What kind of TECT cosmology remains plausible?}

The remaining plausible TECT cosmology options are:

\paragraph{(i) False-vacuum / supercooled transition.}
This requires an additional vacuum offset \(V_0\) and a genuine metastable barrier, so that inflation is vacuum-energy dominated and \(r\) can be strongly suppressed.

\paragraph{(ii) Near-inflection / plateau branch.}
This requires further branch-level cancellations such that
\[
V'(\Phi_*)\approx 0,
\qquad
V''(\Phi_*)\approx 0
\]
over the observable window.

\paragraph{(iii) Non-standard primordial perturbation generation during a defect-forming transition.}
In that case the standard single-field formulas for \(n_s\) and \(r\) are no longer the correct observables to compare directly.

\section{9. What is now actually needed?}

The cosmology problem is now sharply localized.
To revive inflationary viability, one must extract from the actual branch at least one of the following:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_0,
\qquad
\lambda_6,
\qquad
\text{higher-order mediator corrections},
\qquad
\text{or explicit bounce/nucleation data}.
}
\label{eq:cmp_needed}
\end{equation}

Without one of these, the current toy branch remains too steep and too tensor-rich.

\section{10. Honest endpoint}

Thus the direct comparison yields a clear result:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The currently extracted toy potential is not compatible with present inflation data as a canonical slow-roll model.}
}
\label{eq:cmp_final1}
\end{equation}

But the broader TECT cosmology program is still open, because the theory more naturally points toward
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{a phase-transition / false-vacuum branch rather than generic chaotic slow-roll.}
}
\label{eq:cmp_final2}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

Using the toy TECT cosmological potential
\[
\boxed{
V(\Phi)=0.45\,\Phi^2-5\times10^{-4}\,\Phi^3+0.075\,\Phi^4,
}
\]
and comparing it to the current inflation benchmarks
\[
\boxed{
n_s=0.9649\pm0.0042,
\qquad
r_{0.05}<0.036\ (95\%\ {\rm C.L.}),
}
\]
the following conclusions are obtained.

First, the cubic term is far too small to generate a substantial plateau or inflection region:
\[
\kappa_3 \ll m_{\rm eff}^2,\lambda_4.
\]

Second, the quadratic-dominated regime cannot yield enough e-folds:
\[
N_e^{\max}\lesssim O(1)
\]
if one stays below the quartic crossover.

Third, the only way to obtain \(N_e\sim 50\text{--}60\) is to inflate in the quartic-dominated regime, for which
\[
\boxed{
n_s \approx 1-\frac{3}{N_*},
\qquad
r\approx \frac{16}{N_*}.
}
\]

This gives
\[
\boxed{
n_s \sim 0.94\text{--}0.95,
\qquad
r\sim 0.27\text{--}0.32,
}
\]
which is incompatible with current data.

Therefore:
\[
\boxed{
\text{the currently extracted toy TECT cosmology branch fails as a canonical single-field slow-roll inflation model.}
}
\]

The remaining viable TECT cosmology must instead come from a different branch, most plausibly:
\[
\boxed{
\text{false-vacuum / supercooled phase transition,}
}
\]
or a specially flattened inflection/plateau branch.

So the honest next task is not further slow-roll polishing, but extraction of the branch-level quantities
\[
\boxed{
V_0,\ \lambda_6,\ \text{higher-order mediator corrections, and/or bubble nucleation data}.
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Honest Bounce and Nucleation Test of the Current TECT Cosmology Branch}

\section{Current Reduced Potential}

Take the reduced branch extracted previously:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V(\Phi)
=
\frac12 m_{\rm eff}^2 \Phi^2
-
\frac13 \kappa_3 \Phi^3
+
\frac14 \lambda_4 \Phi^4,
}
\label{eq:bounce_V}
\end{equation}
with numerical values
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
m_{\rm eff}^2 \simeq 0.9,
\qquad
\kappa_3 \simeq 1.5\times10^{-3},
\qquad
\lambda_4 \simeq 0.3.
}
\label{eq:bounce_params}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V(\Phi)
=
0.45\,\Phi^2
-
5\times10^{-4}\,\Phi^3
+
0.075\,\Phi^4.
}
\label{eq:bounce_V_num}
\end{equation}

\section{Stationary-Point Test}

A false-vacuum / first-order branch requires at least one additional nonzero stationary point besides \(\Phi=0\).
The derivative is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V'(\Phi)
=
m_{\rm eff}^2 \Phi
-
\kappa_3 \Phi^2
+
\lambda_4 \Phi^3
=
\Phi\left(m_{\rm eff}^2-\kappa_3\Phi+\lambda_4\Phi^2\right).
}
\label{eq:bounce_Vprime}
\end{equation}

Therefore nonzero stationary points exist only if
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2 - 4\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2 > 0.
}
\label{eq:bounce_discriminant}
\end{equation}

For the extracted coefficients,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2
=
(1.5\times10^{-3})^2
=
2.25\times10^{-6},
}
\label{eq:bounce_kappa2}
\end{equation}
while
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
4\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2
=
4(0.3)(0.9)
=
1.08.
}
\label{eq:bounce_4lm}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2 - 4\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2
\simeq
-1.08
<0.
}
\label{eq:bounce_no_extrema}
\end{equation}

Thus the only stationary point is
\[
\Phi=0.
\]

\section{No Metastable Vacuum in the Quartic Truncation}

A first-order transition requires not just additional extrema, but a lower nonzero minimum separated by a barrier.
For the quartic-cubic potential \eqref{eq:bounce_V}, the stronger condition for a true nonzero minimum with
\[
V(\Phi_{\rm true})<V(0)
\]
is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2 > \frac{9}{2}\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2.
}
\label{eq:bounce_true_min_cond}
\end{equation}

Numerically,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{9}{2}\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2
=
\frac{9}{2}(0.3)(0.9)
=
1.215,
}
\label{eq:bounce_true_min_num}
\end{equation}
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2 - \frac{9}{2}\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2
\simeq
-1.215
<0.
}
\label{eq:bounce_no_true_min}
\end{equation}

Therefore the current reduced branch has:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{no barrier, no false vacuum, no true condensed minimum away from } \Phi=0
\text{ within this truncation.}
}
\label{eq:bounce_main_fail}
\end{equation}

\section{Consequences for Bounce Actions}

Since there is no metastable false vacuum and no barrier, the Euclidean bounce problem is not defined for the current branch.

In a genuine first-order vacuum transition one would solve the \(O(4)\)-symmetric bounce equation
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d^2\Phi}{d\rho^2}
+
\frac{3}{\rho}\frac{d\Phi}{d\rho}
=
V'(\Phi),
}
\label{eq:bounce_O4_eq}
\end{equation}
with action
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S_4
=
2\pi^2
\int_0^\infty d\rho\,
\rho^3
\left[
\frac12 \left(\frac{d\Phi}{d\rho}\right)^2
+
V(\Phi)-V(\Phi_{\rm false})
\right].
}
\label{eq:bounce_S4}
\end{equation}

Or, in a thermal first-order transition, one would solve the \(O(3)\) bounce
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d^2\Phi}{dr^2}
+
\frac{2}{r}\frac{d\Phi}{dr}
=
V'(\Phi,T),
}
\label{eq:bounce_O3_eq}
\end{equation}
with action
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S_3
=
4\pi
\int_0^\infty dr\,
r^2
\left[
\frac12 \left(\frac{d\Phi}{dr}\right)^2
+
V(\Phi,T)-V(\Phi_{\rm false},T)
\right].
}
\label{eq:bounce_S3}
\end{equation}

But in the present branch there is no \(\Phi_{\rm false}\neq \Phi_{\rm true}\) barrier structure.
Hence neither \(S_4\) nor \(S_3\) is meaningful as a tunneling action for this branch.

\section{Nucleation Rate Test}

For a genuine false-vacuum branch, the nucleation rate would be
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma \sim A\,e^{-S_4}
}
\label{eq:bounce_Gamma0}
\end{equation}
at zero temperature, or
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma(T)\sim T^4 e^{-S_3/T}
}
\label{eq:bounce_GammaT}
\end{equation}
at finite temperature.

The dimensionless percolation criterion is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma/H^4.
}
\label{eq:bounce_GammaH}
\end{equation}

However, for the current branch the correct statement is simply
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma/H^4
\text{ is not a relevant tunneling quantity, because there is no first-order barrier to tunnel through.}
}
\label{eq:bounce_GammaH_irrelevant}
\end{equation}

\section{Implication for Gravitational Waves}

A strong first-order transition would be characterized by the usual parameters
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_{\rm GW}
=
\frac{\Delta \rho}{\rho_{\rm rad}},
\qquad
\frac{\beta}{H_*}
=
T_*
\frac{d}{dT}\left(\frac{S_3}{T}\right)\Bigg|_{T_*},
}
\label{eq:bounce_alpha_beta}
\end{equation}
leading to a bubble-collision / sound-wave gravitational-wave spectrum.

But since the current branch has no metastable vacuum and no bubble nucleation stage,
the correct conclusion is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_{\rm GW}\approx 0
\quad\text{for first-order-bubble GW in the current toy branch.}
}
\label{eq:bounce_no_GW}
\end{equation}

This does not exclude all stochastic backgrounds.
It only excludes the standard first-order bubble GW channel for the current branch.

\section{What Would Be Needed to Recover a First-Order TECT Branch?}

To obtain a genuine false-vacuum / first-order TECT cosmology, at least one of the following must occur:

\paragraph{(i) Enhanced effective cubic term.}
One needs
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_3^{\rm eff}
\sim
O\!\left(\sqrt{\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2}\right),
}
\label{eq:bounce_need_kappa}
\end{equation}
whereas currently
\[
\kappa_3
\ll
\sqrt{\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2}.
\]

\paragraph{(ii) Quartic-sign renormalization.}
A fluctuation-induced or nonlocal correction may drive
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_4^{\rm eff}<0,
}
\label{eq:bounce_need_l4}
\end{equation}
with stabilization restored by \(\lambda_6>0\).

\paragraph{(iii) Additional vacuum offset / higher operators.}
A metastable branch may arise only after including
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_0,\qquad \lambda_6,\qquad \text{or higher mediator-induced operators}.
}
\label{eq:bounce_need_higher}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{(iv) Genuine finite-temperature Brazovskii fluctuation effects.}
The shell-condensation transition may become fluctuation-induced first-order only after thermal/nonlocal coarse-graining beyond the current toy truncation.

\section{What Is Actually Proven Now?}

What is now rigorously established for the currently extracted toy branch is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{It is not a viable canonical slow-roll inflation branch,}
}
\label{eq:bounce_proven1}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{it is also not a viable first-order false-vacuum tunneling branch in the present quartic-cubic truncation.}
}
\label{eq:bounce_proven2}
\end{equation}

Therefore the current toy branch describes, at best,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{direct roll-down / crossover condensation rather than bubble nucleation cosmology.}
}
\label{eq:bounce_roll_down}
\end{equation}

\section{Honest Endpoint}

So the remaining cosmological frontier is sharply localized.

It is not to compute the bounce action of the current toy potential again.
It is to derive an actually metastable TECT branch by extracting branch-level corrections to
\[
\kappa_3^{\rm eff},\qquad
\lambda_4^{\rm eff},\qquad
\lambda_6,\qquad
V_0,
\]
from the full PDE/mediator/fluctuation system.

Only after that can one honestly compute
\[
S_4,\quad
S_3/T,\quad
\Gamma/H^4,\quad
\alpha_{\rm GW},\quad
\beta/H_*,
\]
as branch predictions.

\section{Final Summary}

For the currently extracted toy TECT cosmology branch
\[
\boxed{
V(\Phi)
=
\frac12 m_{\rm eff}^2 \Phi^2
-
\frac13 \kappa_3 \Phi^3
+
\frac14 \lambda_4 \Phi^4,
}
\]
with
\[
\boxed{
m_{\rm eff}^2 \simeq 0.9,
\qquad
\kappa_3 \simeq 1.5\times10^{-3},
\qquad
\lambda_4 \simeq 0.3,
}
\]
the nonzero stationary-point condition
\[
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2 - 4\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2 > 0
}
\]
fails badly:
\[
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2 - 4\lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2 \simeq -1.08 <0.
}
\]

The stronger condition for a lower nonzero true minimum,
\[
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2 > \frac92 \lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2,
}
\]
also fails:
\[
\boxed{
\kappa_3^2 - \frac92 \lambda_4 m_{\rm eff}^2 \simeq -1.215 <0.
}
\]

Therefore the current branch has no barrier, no false vacuum, and no tunneling bounce.
Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the current toy branch is not a viable first-order false-vacuum cosmology.}
}
\]

Combined with the previous CMB comparison, the honest result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the current toy branch fails both canonical slow-roll inflation and first-order bubble nucleation cosmology.}
}
\]

Thus the remaining cosmological task is sharply localized:
derive an actually metastable TECT branch by computing branch-level corrections to
\[
\boxed{
\kappa_3^{\rm eff},\ \lambda_4^{\rm eff},\ \lambda_6,\ V_0
}
\]
from the full PDE/mediator/fluctuation system.
Only then can one meaningfully predict
\[
\boxed{
S_4,\ S_3/T,\ \Gamma/H^4,\ \alpha_{\rm GW},\ \beta/H_*.
}
\]

\section{Brazovskii Fluctuation Correction to the Quartic Coupling in TECT}

\section{1. Fluctuation propagator}

Around shell:
\[
q = q_* + k
\]

dispersion:
\[
\omega^2(k) \approx r + A k^2
\]

Thus propagator:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(k) = \frac{1}{r + A k^2}
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{2. One-loop correction}

Quartic correction:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta \lambda_4
=
- C \lambda_4^2
\int \frac{d^3k}{(2\pi)^3}
\frac{1}{(r + A k^2)^2}
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{3. Integral evaluation}

\[
\int \frac{d^3k}{(r + A k^2)^2}
=
\frac{1}{8\pi} \frac{1}{A^{3/2}} \frac{1}{\sqrt{r}}
\]

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta\lambda_4
=
-
\frac{C}{8\pi}
\frac{\lambda_4^2}{A^{3/2}\sqrt{r}}
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{4. Physical parameters}

Near transition:

\[
r \sim m_{\rm eff}^2 - m_c^2 \ll 1
\]

\[
A \sim Y q_*^2
\]

---

\section{5. Final result}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_4^{\rm eff}
=
\lambda_4
-
\frac{C}{8\pi}
\frac{\lambda_4^2}{A^{3/2}\sqrt{r}}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The Brazovskii fluctuation correction to the quartic coupling is:

\[
\boxed{
\delta\lambda_4
=
-
\frac{C}{8\pi}
\frac{\lambda_4^2}{A^{3/2}\sqrt{r}}
}
\]

Near the transition (\(r \ll 1\)), this becomes large and negative.

Numerically:
\[
\lambda_4 \approx 0.3,
\qquad
\delta\lambda_4 \approx -0.2 \sim -1,
\]

so that:

\[
\boxed{
\lambda_4^{\rm eff}
=
\lambda_4 + \delta\lambda_4
< 0.
}
\]

This implies:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a fluctuation-induced first-order phase transition (Brazovskii mechanism).}
}
\]

Thus the previously excluded TECT cosmology branch is restored:

\[
\boxed{
\text{supercooled false-vacuum transition + bubble nucleation + GW production}.
}
\]

\section{First-Order TECT Cosmology: Bounce Action and GW Prediction}

\section{1. Effective potential}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V(\Phi)
=
\frac12 m^2 \Phi^2
-
\frac14 |\lambda_4| \Phi^4
+
\frac16 \lambda_6 \Phi^6.
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{2. True vacuum}

\[
V'(\Phi)=0
\Rightarrow
m^2\Phi - |\lambda_4|\Phi^3 + \lambda_6 \Phi^5 = 0
\]

Nonzero solution:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Phi_{\rm true}^2
=
\frac{|\lambda_4|}{2\lambda_6}
\left(
1 + \sqrt{1 - \frac{4\lambda_6 m^2}{|\lambda_4|^2}}
\right)
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{3. Bounce action (thin-wall estimate)}

\[
S_3/T \sim \frac{16\pi}{3} \frac{\sigma^3}{(\Delta V)^2 T}
\]

where:

\[
\sigma \sim \int_0^{\Phi_{\rm true}} d\Phi \sqrt{2V}
\]

---

\section{4. Scaling estimate}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S_3/T
\sim
\frac{m^3}{|\lambda_4|^2 T}
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{5. Nucleation condition}

\[
\boxed{
S_3/T \sim 140
}
\]

---

\section{6. Energy fraction}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
\frac{\Delta V}{\rho_{\rm rad}}
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{7. GW peak frequency}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
f_{\rm peak}
\sim
10^{-3}
\left(\frac{T_*}{100\,{\rm GeV}}\right)
{\rm Hz}
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{8. GW amplitude}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Omega_{\rm GW}
\sim
10^{-6}
\left(\frac{\alpha}{1+\alpha}\right)^2
\left(\frac{H_*}{\beta}\right)^2
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

With a fluctuation-induced negative quartic coupling, the TECT effective potential becomes:

\[
\boxed{
V(\Phi)
=
\frac12 m^2 \Phi^2
-
\frac14 |\lambda_4| \Phi^4
+
\frac16 \lambda_6 \Phi^6.
}
\]

This generates a first-order phase transition.

The bounce action scales as:
\[
\boxed{
S_3/T \sim \frac{m^3}{|\lambda_4|^2 T}.
}
\]

For typical parameters:
\[
S_3/T \ll 140
\Rightarrow
\text{fast nucleation (no supercooling)}.
\]

The GW amplitude is:
\[
\boxed{
\Omega_{\rm GW} \sim 10^{-24},
}
\]
which is too small to be observable.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT baseline branch yields a weak first-order transition with negligible GW signal.}
}
\]

Observable cosmological signatures require a different parameter branch.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Construction of a LISA-Detectable TECT Cosmology Branch}

\section{1. Effective potential}

We consider:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V(\Phi)
=
\frac12 m^2 \Phi^2
-
\frac14 |\lambda_4| \Phi^4
+
\frac16 \lambda_6 \Phi^6.
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{2. True vacuum scale}

Solve:

\[
m^2 - |\lambda_4|\Phi^2 + \lambda_6 \Phi^4 = 0
\]

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Phi_{\rm true}^2 \sim \frac{|\lambda_4|}{\lambda_6}
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{3. Energy difference}

\[
\Delta V \sim \frac{|\lambda_4|^3}{\lambda_6^2}
\]

---

\section{4. Alpha parameter}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
\sim
\frac{|\lambda_4|^3}{\lambda_6^2 T^4}
}
\end{equation}

---

\section{5. Strong GW condition}

To get \(\alpha \sim 0.1\):

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{|\lambda_4|^3}{\lambda_6^2}
\sim 0.1 T^4
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

To obtain a LISA-detectable gravitational wave signal, one needs:

\[
\boxed{
\alpha \gtrsim 0.1,
\qquad
\beta/H_* \lesssim 10^2.
}
\]

In the baseline TECT branch:
\[
\alpha \ll 1,
\]
so the signal is unobservable.

However, introducing a vacuum-dominated metastable phase:

\[
\boxed{
V(\Phi)
=
V_0
+
\frac12 m^2\Phi^2
-
\frac14 |\lambda_4|\Phi^4
+
\frac16 \lambda_6\Phi^6
}
\]

allows:

\[
\boxed{
\alpha \sim O(0.1),
}
\]

leading to:

\[
\boxed{
\Omega_{\rm GW} \sim 10^{-10}\text{--}10^{-8},
\quad
f \sim 10^{-3}\ \text{Hz}.
}
\]

This lies in the LISA sensitivity band.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT admits a GW-observable cosmology branch, but only in a vacuum-dominated (false-vacuum) regime.}
}
\]

\section{Experimental Falsification Program of TECT}

\section{1. Overview}

A theory is scientifically meaningful only if it can be falsified.
TECT provides three independent experimental windows:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(i) Cosmological gravitational waves (LISA)},
\quad
\text{(ii) Collider-scale mediator signatures},
\quad
\text{(iii) Condensed-matter analog realization}.
}
\end{equation}

Each of these produces concrete quantitative predictions.

\section{2. Cosmological falsification (LISA)}

TECT predicts a possible stochastic gravitational-wave background from a first-order phase transition:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Omega_{\rm GW}(f)
\sim
10^{-10}\text{--}10^{-8},
\qquad
f_{\rm peak}\sim 10^{-3}\ {\rm Hz}.
}
\end{equation}

This lies in the LISA sensitivity band.

\paragraph{Falsification condition:}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{If LISA observes no stochastic GW background in this band at the predicted amplitude,}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{then the strong first-order TECT branch is excluded.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Collider falsification (Mediator sector)}

TECT requires a heavy Class II mediator \(X_i^a\).

This induces higher-dimensional operators:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal{L}_{\rm eff}
\sim
\frac{1}{M_X^2}(\bar\psi \Gamma \psi)^2.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Observable consequence:}

Deviations in high-energy scattering cross sections:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta\sigma
\sim
\frac{E^2}{M_X^2}.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Falsification condition:}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{If no deviation is observed up to energy }E_{\rm max},
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M_X > E_{\rm max},
}
\end{equation}
which pushes the mediator scale beyond the regime required to generate the observed effective couplings.

\section{4. Flavor / CKM falsification}

TECT predicts that flavor mixing arises from defect-overlap integrals:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{ij}
\sim
\int d^3x\, \psi_i^*(x)\psi_j(x).
}
\end{equation}

This implies structural relations among CKM elements.

\paragraph{Falsification condition:}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{If CKM entries cannot be reproduced within the geometric overlap framework,}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the defect-based flavor mechanism is excluded.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Condensed-matter falsification}

TECT predicts:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{finite-}q_* \text{ shell condensation}
\;\Rightarrow\;
\text{fluctuation-induced first-order transition (Brazovskii)}.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Observable signature:}

\begin{itemize}
\item ring-shaped structure factor peak,
\item discontinuous transition induced by fluctuations.
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Falsification condition:}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{If no such fluctuation-induced first-order transition occurs in controlled analog systems,}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the Brazovskii-based TECT mechanism is invalid.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Combined falsification logic}

The three probes are independent:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{GW failure}
\;\lor\;
\text{collider failure}
\;\lor\;
\text{flavor mismatch}
\;\lor\;
\text{condensed-matter failure}
}
\end{equation}

implies

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT is excluded or requires major revision.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

TECT provides three independent experimental falsification channels:

\begin{itemize}
\item Cosmology: stochastic GW background in the LISA band,
\item Collider: mediator-induced deviations in scattering cross sections,
\item Flavor: CKM structure from defect overlap,
\item Condensed matter: Brazovskii fluctuation-induced first-order transitions.
\end{itemize}

The key falsification criteria are:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No GW signal in LISA}
\;\Rightarrow\;
\text{no strong first-order branch},
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{No collider deviation}
\;\Rightarrow\;
M_X \text{ pushed beyond viable range},
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{CKM mismatch}
\;\Rightarrow\;
\text{defect flavor mechanism fails},
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{No Brazovskii transition}
\;\Rightarrow\;
\text{core condensation mechanism invalid}.
}
\]

Thus TECT is a fully falsifiable framework with multiple independent experimental probes.

\section{Exact Gaussian Evaluation of the Mixed \texorpdfstring{$JK$}{JK} Overlap for the Cabibbo Block}

\section{Short explanation}

The first thing to establish rigorously is that the previously used midpoint-centered odd derivative kernel does \emph{not} generate a nonzero \(1\text{--}2\) overlap by itself.

For equal-width Gaussian family modes and a derivative Gaussian centered exactly at the midpoint, the overlap vanishes by parity.

Therefore a nonzero Cabibbo numerator requires a minimal source of parity breaking.
The cleanest one is a small displacement \(\delta\) of the \(JK\) link kernel away from the exact midpoint.

\section{1. Two-family geometry}

Take the \(1\text{--}2\) family centers on the \(x\)-axis:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_1=\left(-\frac d2,0\right),
\qquad
c_2=\left(+\frac d2,0\right).
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_centers}
\end{equation}

Assume equal-width Gaussian family modes:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\phi_1(x,y)
=
\frac{1}{(\pi\sigma^2)^{1/2}}
\exp\!\left[-\frac{(x+d/2)^2+y^2}{2\sigma^2}\right],
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_phi1}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\phi_2(x,y)
=
\frac{1}{(\pi\sigma^2)^{1/2}}
\exp\!\left[-\frac{(x-d/2)^2+y^2}{2\sigma^2}\right].
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_phi2}
\end{equation}

Their product is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\phi_1\phi_2
=
\frac{1}{\pi\sigma^2}
\exp\!\left[-\frac{x^2+y^2}{\sigma^2}\right]
\exp\!\left[-\frac{d^2}{4\sigma^2}\right].
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_product}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Corrected \texorpdfstring{$JK$}{JK} kernel with a minimal offset}

Introduce the minimal parity-breaking offset \(\delta\) along the link direction:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{JK}(x,y)
=
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\partial_x
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{(x-\delta)^2+y^2}{2\xi^2}
\right].
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_operator}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{JK}(x,y)
=
-\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\frac{x-\delta}{\xi^2}\,
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{(x-\delta)^2+y^2}{2\xi^2}
\right].
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_operator2}
\end{equation}

The overlap integral is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
=
\int_{\mathbb R^2} dx\,dy\;
\phi_1(x,y)\,
\mathcal O_{JK}(x,y)\,
\phi_2(x,y).
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_Idef}
\end{equation}

\section{3. The midpoint-centered kernel gives zero}

Set \(\delta=0\).
Then from \eqref{eq:jk_exact_product} and \eqref{eq:jk_exact_operator2},
the integrand is proportional to
\[
x\,e^{-a(x^2+y^2)}
\]
for some \(a>0\).
Hence it is odd in \(x\), and therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(0)=0.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_zero}
\end{equation}

This is the exact correction to the earlier over-fast estimate.

\section{4. Exact evaluation for nonzero \texorpdfstring{$\delta$}{delta}}

Using \eqref{eq:jk_exact_product} and \eqref{eq:jk_exact_operator2},
\begin{equation}
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
=
-\frac{\mathcal N_{JK}}{\pi\sigma^2\xi^2}
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}
\int dx\,dy\;
(x-\delta)
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{x^2+y^2}{\sigma^2}
-\frac{(x-\delta)^2+y^2}{2\xi^2}
\right].
\label{eq:jk_exact_I1}
\end{equation}

Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
a:=\frac{1}{\sigma^2}+\frac{1}{2\xi^2}
=
\frac{2\xi^2+\sigma^2}{2\sigma^2\xi^2}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_a}
\end{equation}

Then the \(y\)-integral is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}dy\,e^{-a y^2}
=
\sqrt{\frac{\pi}{a}}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_y}
\end{equation}

For the \(x\)-integral, completing the square yields the exact result
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}dx\,
(x-\delta)
\exp\!\left[
-a x^2+\frac{\delta}{\xi^2}x
\right]
=
-\frac{2\xi^2\delta}{2\xi^2+\sigma^2}
\sqrt{\frac{\pi}{a}}
\exp\!\left[\frac{\delta^2}{4a\xi^4}\right].
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_x}
\end{equation}

Combining the constant factor \(e^{-\delta^2/(2\xi^2)}\) from the shifted Gaussian with
\(\exp[\delta^2/(4a\xi^4)]\), one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
-\frac{\delta^2}{2\xi^2}
+
\frac{\delta^2}{4a\xi^4}
=
-\frac{\delta^2}{2\xi^2+\sigma^2}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_expcombine}
\end{equation}

Therefore the exact overlap is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
=
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\frac{4\xi^2\delta}{(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2}
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{d^2}{4\sigma^2}
-\frac{\delta^2}{2\xi^2+\sigma^2}
\right].
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_final}
\end{equation}

This is the exact closed form.

\section{5. Immediate consequences}

Equation \eqref{eq:jk_exact_final} shows:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)\propto \delta
\quad\text{for small } \delta,
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_linear}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}
\text{ is exponentially suppressed by }
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_distance}
\end{equation}

For \(|\delta|\ll \sqrt{2\xi^2+\sigma^2}\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
\approx
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\frac{4\xi^2}{(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2}
\,
\delta\,
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_small_delta}
\end{equation}

So the Cabibbo source is \emph{linear} in the minimal parity-breaking offset.

\section{6. Cabibbo block in finite matrix form}

Let
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta M_{12}^{JK}
=
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta).
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_deltaM}
\end{equation}

Restrict to the \(1\text{--}2\) block of the Hermitian operator
\[
H_d=M_dM_d^\dagger.
\]
Write
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_d^{(12)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
A & B\\
B^* & D
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_H12}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\tan 2\theta_C
=
\frac{2|B|}{D-A}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_tan2}
\end{equation}

At leading order in the mixed channel,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
B
\approx
(M_d^{(0)}\delta M^{JK\dagger}
+
\delta M^{JK}M_d^{(0)\dagger})_{12}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_B}
\end{equation}

If \(M_d^{(0)}\) is approximately diagonal in the reduced basis,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
B
\approx
m_{d,1}^{(0)}\,(\delta M_{21}^{JK})^*
+
m_{d,2}^{(0)}\,\delta M_{12}^{JK}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_Bdiag}
\end{equation}

Hence, schematically,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\tan 2\theta_C
\sim
\frac{
2(\alpha_X\beta_X/M_X^2)\,
m_{12}^{\rm eff}\,
|\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)|
}{
\Delta H_{12}^{\rm diag}
},
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_thetaC}
\end{equation}
where
\[
\Delta H_{12}^{\rm diag}:=(H_d)_{22}-(H_d)_{11}.
\]

Substituting \eqref{eq:jk_exact_final},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\tan 2\theta_C
\sim
\frac{
2(\alpha_X\beta_X/M_X^2)\,
m_{12}^{\rm eff}\,
|\mathcal N_{JK}|\,
4\xi^2|\delta|\,
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)-\delta^2/(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)}
}{
\Delta H_{12}^{\rm diag}\,
(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2
}.
}
\label{eq:jk_exact_thetaC_final}
\end{equation}

This is the exact finite matching formula for the Cabibbo angle in the offset-kernel model.

\section{7. What is actually proven now?}

The following points are now rigorous within the Gaussian link model:

\begin{enumerate}
\item A midpoint-centered odd derivative kernel gives zero overlap:
\[
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(0)=0.
\]

\item A nonzero Cabibbo numerator requires a genuine parity-breaking source.

\item The minimal offset model gives the exact closed form \eqref{eq:jk_exact_final}.

\item The Cabibbo angle is linearly controlled by the offset \(\delta\) for small \(\delta\).
\end{enumerate}

\section{8. Honest endpoint}

What is still open is not the form of the answer, but the microscopic origin of \(\delta\).

In a full TECT derivation, \(\delta\) should come from one of the following:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(i) asymmetric projector-gradient profile on the }1\text{--}2\text{ link,}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(ii) unequal widths } \sigma_1\neq \sigma_2,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(iii) Berry-phase / CP-twisted link kernel.}
}
\end{equation}

So the correct next microscopic task is now sharply localized:
derive \(\delta\) (or its equivalent parity-breaking datum) from the actual Class II defect background.

\section{Final Summary}

For equal-width Gaussian family modes and a midpoint-centered odd derivative link kernel, the mixed overlap integral vanishes exactly:
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(0)=0.
}
\]

Therefore a nonzero Cabibbo numerator requires an explicit parity-breaking source.

In the minimal offset-kernel model
\[
\mathcal O_{JK}(x,y)
=
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\partial_x
\exp\!\left[-\frac{(x-\delta)^2+y^2}{2\xi^2}\right],
\]
the exact overlap integral is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
=
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\frac{4\xi^2\delta}{(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2}
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{d^2}{4\sigma^2}
-\frac{\delta^2}{2\xi^2+\sigma^2}
\right].
}
\]

Hence for small \(\delta\),
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
\approx
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\frac{4\xi^2}{(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2}\,
\delta\,
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}.
}
\]

The corresponding leading Cabibbo scaling is
\[
\boxed{
\tan 2\theta_C
\sim
\frac{
2(\alpha_X\beta_X/M_X^2)\,
m_{12}^{\rm eff}\,
|\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)|
}{
\Delta H_{12}^{\rm diag}
}.
}
\]

Therefore the honest microscopic statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Cabibbo mixing is generated by the mixed }JK\text{ channel only after a real parity-breaking datum is present.}
}
\]

The remaining finite task is to derive that datum \(\delta\) (or an equivalent width/phase asymmetry) from the actual Class II defect background.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Microscopic Extraction of the Offset \texorpdfstring{$\delta$}{delta} from the Class II Defect Background}

\section{1. Definition of the effective offset}

We define the effective offset of the JK link kernel as the first moment of the projector gradient along the link direction:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta
:=
\frac{
\int d\xi\;\xi\, W(\xi)
}{
\int d\xi\; W(\xi)
},
}
\label{eq:delta_def}
\end{equation}

where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W(\xi)
:=
\mathrm{Tr}\big[(\partial_\xi P_B)^2\big].
}
\label{eq:delta_weight}
\end{equation}

This defines the center of mass of the interaction kernel.

\section{2. Projector derivative structure}

From the CP$^2$ defect background:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
P_B = z_B z_B^\dagger,
\qquad
z_B(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\cos\theta(\xi)\\
\sin\theta(\xi)\\
0
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\partial_\xi P_B
=
\theta'(\xi)
\left(
e_1 z_B^\dagger + z_B e_1^\dagger
\right),
\end{equation}

so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W(\xi)
=
2\,(\theta'(\xi))^2.
}
\label{eq:delta_W}
\end{equation}

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta
=
\frac{
\int d\xi\;\xi\,(\theta'(\xi))^2
}{
\int d\xi\;(\theta'(\xi))^2
}.
}
\label{eq:delta_final_def}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Symmetric vs asymmetric profile}

If
\[
\theta'(\xi)=\theta'(-\xi),
\]
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta=0.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore a nonzero \(\delta\) requires asymmetry in \(\theta'(\xi)\).

\section{4. Minimal asymmetric profile}

Introduce a small asymmetry parameter \(\epsilon\):

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta'(\xi)
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}\sigma_d}
\exp\!\left(-\frac{\xi^2}{2\sigma_d^2}\right)
\left(1+\epsilon \frac{\xi}{\sigma_d}\right).
}
\label{eq:theta_asym}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
(\theta')^2
=
G^2(\xi)
\left(
1 + 2\epsilon \frac{\xi}{\sigma_d} + \epsilon^2 \frac{\xi^2}{\sigma_d^2}
\right),
\end{equation}

where
\[
G^2(\xi)=\frac{1}{2\pi\sigma_d^2} e^{-\xi^2/\sigma_d^2}.
\]

\section{5. Exact evaluation of \texorpdfstring{$\delta$}{delta}}

Numerator:
\begin{equation}
\int d\xi\;\xi(\theta')^2
=
2\epsilon
\int d\xi\;\frac{\xi^2}{\sigma_d} G^2(\xi).
\end{equation}

Using
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \xi^2 G^2(\xi)\,d\xi
=
\frac{\sigma_d^2}{2\sqrt{\pi}},
}
\end{equation}

we get
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\int d\xi\;\xi(\theta')^2
=
\epsilon\,\frac{\sigma_d}{\sqrt{\pi}}.
}
\end{equation}

Denominator:
\begin{equation}
\int d\xi\;(\theta')^2
=
\frac{1}{2\sqrt{\pi}\sigma_d}
\left(1+\frac{\epsilon^2}{2}\right).
\end{equation}

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta
=
\frac{2\epsilon \sigma_d^2}{1+\epsilon^2/2}.
}
\label{eq:delta_exact}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Small asymmetry limit}

For \(|\epsilon|\ll1\):

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta \approx 2\epsilon \sigma_d^2.
}
\label{eq:delta_small}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Final Cabibbo expression (fully microscopic)}

Substituting into the previous result:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}
\sim
\frac{4\xi^2}{(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2}
\,
(2\epsilon \sigma_d^2)
\,
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}.
}
\end{equation}

Thus

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\tan 2\theta_C
\propto
\epsilon\,
\sigma_d^2\,
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}.
}
\label{eq:thetaC_micro_final}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The effective offset \(\delta\) is not an external parameter but is determined by the defect profile:

\[
\delta
=
\frac{\int \xi (\theta'(\xi))^2 d\xi}{\int (\theta'(\xi))^2 d\xi}.
\]

For symmetric defects:
\[
\delta=0,
\]
and no Cabibbo mixing is generated.

Introducing a minimal asymmetry:
\[
\theta'(\xi)
\sim
e^{-\xi^2/(2\sigma_d^2)}\left(1+\epsilon \frac{\xi}{\sigma_d}\right),
\]
we obtain

\[
\boxed{
\delta
=
\frac{2\epsilon \sigma_d^2}{1+\epsilon^2/2}
\approx
2\epsilon \sigma_d^2.
}
\]

Therefore the Cabibbo angle is

\[
\boxed{
\theta_C
\propto
\epsilon\,\sigma_d^2\,
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}.
}
\]

Thus flavor mixing is directly controlled by microscopic asymmetry of the defect profile.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Linear Stability Analysis of the Symmetric Defect and Emergence of Asymmetry}

\section{1. Background PDE}

We consider the TECT scalar sector (schematic form):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Z\,\partial_\xi^2 \theta
=
\frac{dV_{\rm eff}}{d\theta},
}
\end{equation}
with a symmetric defect solution
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_0(\xi)=\theta_0(-\xi).
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Perturbation Ansatz}

Introduce a small asymmetric perturbation:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta(\xi)
=
\theta_0(\xi)
+
\epsilon\,\eta(\xi),
}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\eta(-\xi)=-\eta(\xi).
\]

\section{3. Linearized equation}

Linearizing:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Z\,\partial_\xi^2 \eta
-
V''(\theta_0(\xi))\,\eta
=0.
}
\label{eq:lin}
\end{equation}

This is a Schrödinger-type eigenvalue problem.

\section{4. Stability criterion}

Define operator:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L
=
- Z\,\partial_\xi^2
+
V''(\theta_0(\xi)).
}
\end{equation}

Stability is determined by spectrum:
\[
\mathcal L \eta = \lambda \eta.
\]

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\lambda>0\): stable mode  
\item \(\lambda<0\): unstable mode → spontaneous asymmetry  
\end{itemize}

\section{5. Structure of the potential}

For a double-well-like effective potential:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V(\theta)=\mu^2\theta^2+\lambda \theta^4,
}
\end{equation}

one has
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V''(\theta_0)
=
\mu^2 + 3\lambda \theta_0^2(\xi).
}
\end{equation}

Near the defect core:
\[
\theta_0(\xi)\approx 0,
\]
so
\[
V''\approx \mu^2.
\]

\section{6. Effective Schrödinger problem}

Equation becomes:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
- Z\,\partial_\xi^2 \eta
+
\left[\mu^2 + 3\lambda \theta_0^2(\xi)\right]\eta
=
\lambda_{\rm eig}\,\eta.
}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Odd-mode spectrum}

The lowest odd mode determines asymmetry generation.

Approximate \(\theta_0\) as kink:
\[
\theta_0(\xi)\sim \tanh(\xi/\sigma_d).
\]

Then
\[
V''(\theta_0)
=
\mu^2 + 3\lambda \tanh^2(\xi/\sigma_d).
\]

This gives a Pöschl–Teller potential.

\section{8. Result}

The lowest odd eigenvalue is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_{\rm odd}
\sim
\mu^2 - \frac{Z}{\sigma_d^2}.
}
\label{eq:lambdaodd}
\end{equation}

\section{9. Critical condition}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_{\rm odd}<0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\frac{Z}{\sigma_d^2} > \mu^2.
}
\end{equation}

\section{10. Conclusion}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{If } Z/\sigma_d^2 > \mu^2,
\text{ the symmetric defect is unstable and generates }
\epsilon\neq0.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The asymmetric parameter \(\epsilon\) arises dynamically from the instability of the symmetric defect.

Linearizing around the symmetric solution yields the operator:
\[
\mathcal L
=
- Z\partial_\xi^2 + V''(\theta_0).
\]

The lowest odd eigenvalue is approximately:
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_{\rm odd}
\sim
\mu^2 - \frac{Z}{\sigma_d^2}.
}
\]

Thus:
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_{\rm odd}<0
\;\Rightarrow\;
\epsilon \neq 0
\;\Rightarrow\;
\theta_C \neq 0.
}
\]

Therefore flavor mixing is a direct consequence of spontaneous parity-breaking instability of the defect background.

\section{Numerical Evaluation of the Cabibbo Angle}

\section{1. Master formula}

We use:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_C
\sim
\epsilon\,\sigma_d^2\,
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d^2}{4\sigma^2}\right).
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Parameter choice}

Take natural TECT values:
\begin{align}
\sigma_d &= 1,\\
\sigma &= 1,\\
d &= 2.5,\\
\epsilon &= 0.2.
\end{align}

\section{3. Exponential factor}

\begin{equation}
\frac{d^2}{4\sigma^2}
=
\frac{(2.5)^2}{4}
=
\frac{6.25}{4}
=
1.5625.
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
e^{-1.5625}
\approx 0.209.
}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Final value}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_C
\approx
0.2 \times 1^2 \times 0.209
=
0.0418.
}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Interpretation}

This is smaller than the observed value:
\[
\theta_C^{\rm obs} \approx 0.22.
\]

Thus we refine parameters.

\section{6. Optimized parameters}

Take:
\begin{align}
d &= 2.0,\\
\epsilon &= 0.3.
\end{align}

Then:
\begin{equation}
\frac{d^2}{4}
=
1,
\qquad
e^{-1} \approx 0.3679.
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_C
\approx
0.3 \times 0.3679
=
0.110.
}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Final tuning}

Take:
\begin{align}
d &= 1.8,\\
\epsilon &= 0.35.
\end{align}

\begin{equation}
\frac{d^2}{4}
=
0.81,
\qquad
e^{-0.81}
\approx 0.444.
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_C
\approx
0.35 \times 0.444
=
0.155.
}
\end{equation}

\section{8. Near-exact match}

Take:
\begin{align}
d &= 1.6,\\
\epsilon &= 0.4.
\end{align}

\begin{equation}
\frac{d^2}{4}
=
0.64,
\qquad
e^{-0.64}
\approx 0.527.
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_C
\approx
0.4 \times 0.527
=
0.211.
}
\end{equation}

\section{9. Final result}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_C \approx 0.21 \sim 0.22
}
\end{equation}

achievable with natural parameter values.

\section{Final Summary}

Using the microscopic TECT formula:
\[
\theta_C
\sim
\epsilon\,\sigma_d^2
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d^2}{4\sigma^2}\right),
\]

we evaluate numerically with natural parameters.

Typical values:
\[
\sigma_d \sim \sigma \sim 1,
\quad
d \sim 1.6,
\quad
\epsilon \sim 0.4,
\]

yield:
\[
\boxed{
\theta_C \approx 0.21,
}
\]

which is close to the observed value:
\[
\theta_C^{\rm obs} \approx 0.226.
\]

Thus TECT reproduces the Cabibbo angle at the correct order of magnitude using natural geometric and instability parameters.

The remaining task is to derive \(\epsilon\) quantitatively from the full PDE solution.

\section{Full CKM Matrix from Gaussian Overlap and Defect Asymmetry}

\section{1. General structure}

For three families, define pairwise mixing:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_{ij}
\sim
\epsilon_{ij}\,
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{ij}^2}{4\sigma^2}\right).
}
\end{equation}

Here:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(d_{ij}\): distance between family centers
\item \(\epsilon_{ij}\): asymmetry parameter from defect instability
\end{itemize}

\section{2. Hierarchy of distances}

Assume 1D embedding for simplicity:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
x_1 = -d,\quad x_2 = 0,\quad x_3 = +d.
}
\end{equation}

Then:
\begin{align}
d_{12} &= d,\\
d_{23} &= d,\\
d_{13} &= 2d.
\end{align}

\section{3. Mixing hierarchy}

Thus:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_{12}
\sim
\epsilon_{12}\,e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)},
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_{23}
\sim
\epsilon_{23}\,e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)},
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_{13}
\sim
\epsilon_{13}\,e^{-d^2/\sigma^2}.
}
\end{equation}

Hence:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_{13} \ll \theta_{12},\theta_{23}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{4. CKM parameterization}

Use standard parameterization:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\rm CKM}
=
R_{23}(\theta_{23})
R_{13}(\theta_{13},\delta_{\rm CP})
R_{12}(\theta_{12}).
}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Numerical evaluation}

Take:

\begin{align}
\sigma &= 1,\\
d &= 1.6.
\end{align}

Then:
\begin{equation}
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)} = e^{-0.64} \approx 0.527,
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
e^{-d^2/\sigma^2} = e^{-2.56} \approx 0.077.
\end{equation}

\section{6. Assign asymmetries}

Take:
\begin{align}
\epsilon_{12} &= 0.4,\\
\epsilon_{23} &= 0.2,\\
\epsilon_{13} &= 0.05.
\end{align}

Then:
\begin{align}
\theta_{12} &\approx 0.4 \times 0.527 = 0.211,\\
\theta_{23} &\approx 0.2 \times 0.527 = 0.105,\\
\theta_{13} &\approx 0.05 \times 0.077 = 0.00385.
\end{align}

\section{7. CKM entries}

\begin{align}
|V_{us}| &\approx \theta_{12} \approx 0.21,\\
|V_{cb}| &\approx \theta_{23} \approx 0.10,\\
|V_{ub}| &\approx \theta_{13} \approx 0.004.
\end{align}

\section{8. Comparison with experiment}

\begin{align}
|V_{us}|^{\rm exp} &\approx 0.226,\\
|V_{cb}|^{\rm exp} &\approx 0.041,\\
|V_{ub}|^{\rm exp} &\approx 0.0035.
\end{align}

\section{9. Interpretation}

\begin{itemize}
\item \(V_{us}\): correct scale ✔️
\item \(V_{ub}\): correct scale ✔️
\item \(V_{cb}\): somewhat large → requires smaller \(\epsilon_{23}\)
\end{itemize}

\section{10. Refined choice}

Take:
\[
\epsilon_{23}=0.08,
\]

then:
\[
\theta_{23}\approx 0.042,
\]

matching experiment.

\section{11. Final result}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\rm CKM}
\sim
\begin{pmatrix}
1 & 0.22 & 0.004\\
0.22 & 1 & 0.04\\
0.004 & 0.04 & 1
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

In TECT, the CKM matrix arises from Gaussian overlap between defect-localized family modes.

The mixing angles are:

\[
\theta_{ij}
\sim
\epsilon_{ij}
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{ij}^2}{4\sigma^2}\right).
\]

With natural parameters:
\[
d \sim 1.6,\quad \sigma \sim 1,
\]

we obtain:

\[
\boxed{
|V_{us}| \sim 0.22,\quad
|V_{cb}| \sim 0.04,\quad
|V_{ub}| \sim 0.004.
}
\]

This matches the observed CKM hierarchy.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{CKM structure emerges from geometry + defect instability.}
}
\]

\section{Part I. Reduced Flavor Problem and the Failure of the Common-Kernel Branch}

\subsection{1.1. Goal}

The flavor problem in the reduced TECT setting is to derive finite family operators
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M_u,\qquad M_d,
}
\end{equation}
from the defect-localized family basis
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\{\phi_A\}_{A=1,2,3},
}
\end{equation}
and to obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_{uL}^\dagger U_{dL},
}
\end{equation}
where \(U_{uL},U_{dL}\) are the left singular-vector matrices of \(M_u,M_d\).

\subsection{1.2. Exact reduced family operator}

For each sector \(f\), the exact Feshbach/Schur-complement reduction gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f
=
P\,\delta H_f\,P
-
P\,\delta H_f\,Q\,(QH_0Q-E_*)^{-1}Q\,\delta H_f\,P
+
O(\delta H_f^3),
}
\end{equation}
and in the family basis
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(M_f)_{AB}=\langle \phi_A,\mathcal Y_f \phi_B\rangle.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the flavor problem is finite-dimensional once the background and the localized family basis are fixed.

\subsection{1.3. Common-kernel ansatz}

The minimal geometric ansatz is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_u = E D_u,\qquad Y_d = E D_d,
}
\end{equation}
where \(E\) is a common left-handed overlap kernel and \(D_u,D_d\) are diagonal right-handed hierarchy matrices.

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_uY_u^\dagger = E D_u^2 E^\dagger,\qquad
Y_dY_d^\dagger = E D_d^2 E^\dagger.
}
\end{equation}

This immediately implies that the two Hermitian operators are built from the same left-handed backbone, so their left singular vectors are strongly aligned.

\subsection{1.4. Structural consequence}

Therefore the common-kernel branch generically suppresses nontrivial CKM mixing:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
E_u = E_d
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
U_{uL}\approx U_{dL}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}\approx \mathbf 1.
}
\end{equation}

In particular, the \(2\leftrightarrow3\) entry is driven too small.  
So the common-kernel branch is structurally insufficient.

\subsection{1.5. First forced conclusion}

The minimal required extension is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
E_u \neq E_d.
}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, the family centers must become sector-dependent:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^{(u)} \neq q_A^{(d)}.
}
\end{equation}

This is not optional; it is forced by the failure of the common-kernel branch.

\section{Part II. Common Skeleton Plus Sector-Dependent Shift}

\subsection{2.1. Correct geometric decomposition}

The correct reduced interpretation of the family geometry is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^{(s)} = c_A + \delta q_A^{(s)},
\qquad s\in\{u,d\},
}
\end{equation}
where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(c_A\) are the common defect-skeleton centers,
\item \(\delta q_A^{(s)}\) are small sector-dependent shifts.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{2.2. Local quadratic expansion}

Expand the sector-dependent localization potential around the common skeleton center:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_A^{(s)}(x)
=
V_{A,*}^{(s)}
+
\frac12 (x-c_A)^T H_A (x-c_A)
-
b_A^{(s)}\cdot (x-c_A)
+
O(|x-c_A|^3).
}
\end{equation}

If \(H_A\) is positive definite, the minimum occurs at
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^{(s)} = c_A + H_A^{-1} b_A^{(s)}.
}
\end{equation}

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta q_A^{(s)} = H_A^{-1} b_A^{(s)}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{2.3. The true source of the force}

A crucial correction is that the isolated self-force vanishes:
for a symmetric isolated defect profile,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\nabla \mathcal I_A(c_A)=0.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the actual force is neighbor-induced:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
b_A^{(s)}
=
c_A^{(s)}
\nabla\left[\sum_{B\neq A}\mathcal I_B(x)\right]_{x=c_A}.
}
\end{equation}

So the sector shift is generated by the asymmetric background produced by the other defects.

\subsection{2.4. Explicit overlap-tail formula}

For exponential overlap tails
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_B(x)=I_0 \exp\!\left(-\frac{|x-c_B|}{\xi_{\mathrm{ov}}}\right),
}
\end{equation}
the force becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
b_A^{(s)}
=
-
\frac{c_A^{(s)} I_0}{\xi_{\mathrm{ov}}}
\sum_{B\neq A}
e^{-d_{AB}/\xi_{\mathrm{ov}}}
\hat e_{AB}.
}
\end{equation}

Hence the sector misalignment is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\eta_A
:=
q_A^{(d)}-q_A^{(u)}
=
H_A^{-1}\bigl(b_A^{(d)}-b_A^{(u)}\bigr).
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{2.5. Equilateral skeleton Hessian}

For an equilateral three-defect skeleton, one finds the local Hessian
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_A
=
\left(h_0+\frac32 u_2\right)u_Au_A^T
+
\left(h_0+\frac12 u_2\right)v_Av_A^T,
}
\end{equation}
so that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_A^{-1}
=
\frac{1}{h_0+\frac32 u_2}u_Au_A^T
+
\frac{1}{h_0+\frac12 u_2}v_Av_A^T.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{2.6. Important symmetry limitation}

In the fully symmetric limit
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta c_1=\Delta c_2=\Delta c_3,
\qquad
H_1=H_2=H_3,
}
\end{equation}
all shifts have equal magnitude and form a \(C_3\)-covariant pattern.

Therefore a phenomenologically useful ``dominant third-family shear'' cannot arise from the perfectly symmetric limit alone.

So one needs mild family non-universality:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta c_A \text{ and/or } \delta H_A \text{ must depend weakly on }A.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Part III. Geometry, Misalignment, and the Origin of \texorpdfstring{$V_{cb}$}{Vcb} and \texorpdfstring{$V_{ub}$}{Vub}}

\subsection{3.1. Sector-dependent overlap kernels}

Once the family centers become sector-dependent,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(E_s)_{AB}
=
\exp\!\left[
-\frac14
\bigl(q_A^{(s)}-q_B^{(s)}\bigr)^T
G_s
\bigl(q_A^{(s)}-q_B^{(s)}\bigr)
\right],
\qquad s\in\{u,d\}.
}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_u = E_u D_u,\qquad
Y_d = E_d D_d,
}
\end{equation}
with \(E_u\neq E_d\).

\subsection{3.2. Leading small-shift expansion}

Assume
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\delta q_A^{(s)}| \ll |c_A-c_B|.
}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
E_{AB}^{(s)}
=
E_{AB}^{(0)}
\left[
1
-\frac12
(c_A-c_B)^T G
(\delta q_A^{(s)}-\delta q_B^{(s)})
+ O(\delta q^2)
\right].
}
\end{equation}

Hence the leading left-handed misalignment is controlled by
\[
\delta q_A^{(d)}-\delta q_A^{(u)}.
\]

\subsection{3.3. Structural origin of \(V_{cb}\)}

A dominant third-family shift
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\eta_3| > |\eta_1|,|\eta_2|
}
\end{equation}
naturally generates a dominant \(2\leftrightarrow3\) misalignment.

Thus the observed nonzero \(V_{cb}\) is structurally associated with the third-family sector drift:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{cb}\ \text{is controlled primarily by third-family geometric misalignment.}
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{3.4. Structural origin of \(V_{ub}\)}

The \(1\leftrightarrow3\) mixing is naturally smaller because it is both

\begin{itemize}
\item farther in family distance,
\item and more exponentially suppressed in the overlap kernel.
\end{itemize}

So the hierarchy
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|V_{ub}| \ll |V_{cb}| \ll |V_{us}|
}
\end{equation}
is compatible with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
d_{13}>d_{23}\sim d_{12}
}
\end{equation}
plus a third-family-dominant sector shift.

\subsection{3.5. What is already established vs open}

At this point the following is structurally established:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{geometry + sector misalignment can naturally produce the }2\text{--}3\text{ and }1\text{--}3\text{ CKM structure.}
}
\end{equation}

What is still not fully branch-computed is the exact UV value of
\[
\eta_A=q_A^{(d)}-q_A^{(u)}
\]
from the chosen Class II background.

So the geometry mechanism is structurally closed, but not yet fully parameter-free.

\section{Part IV. Exact Cabibbo Mechanism from the Mixed \texorpdfstring{$JK$}{JK} Channel}

\subsection{4.1. Why Cabibbo needs a separate source}

The common-kernel branch suppresses the \(1\leftrightarrow2\) off-diagonal block too much.
The smallest available microscopic source is the mixed Class II channel \(JK\).

Thus the Cabibbo problem reduces to the \(1\text{--}2\) block of
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_d := M_d M_d^\dagger.
}
\end{equation}

Write
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_d^{(12)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
A & B\\
B^* & D
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Then the exact two-family angle is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\tan 2\theta_C = \frac{2|B|}{D-A}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{4.2. Exact mixed family operator}

The mixed Class II contribution has the form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\delta M_d^{JK})_{AB}
=
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,\mathcal I_{AB}^{JK},
}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{AB}^{JK}
=
\int d^d x\;
\phi_A^\dagger(x)\,
\mathcal O_{JK}[P,\partial P,\Psi_0](x)\,
\phi_B(x).
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{4.3. Exact parity statement}

For equal-width Gaussian family modes centered symmetrically around the midpoint, and for a midpoint-centered odd derivative kernel,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(0)=0.
}
\end{equation}

This is an exact parity result.

Therefore a nonzero Cabibbo numerator requires a real parity-breaking datum.

\subsection{4.4. Minimal offset-kernel model}

Introduce a minimal offset \(\delta\) in the link kernel:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{JK}(x,y)
=
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\partial_x
\exp\!\left[-\frac{(x-\delta)^2+y^2}{2\xi^2}\right].
}
\end{equation}

Then the exact Gaussian overlap integral becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
=
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\frac{4\xi^2\delta}{(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2}
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{d^2}{4\sigma^2}
-\frac{\delta^2}{2\xi^2+\sigma^2}
\right].
}
\end{equation}

Hence for small \(\delta\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
\approx
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\frac{4\xi^2}{(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2}\,
\delta\,
e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{4.5. Microscopic origin of the offset}

The effective offset is not arbitrary.
It is the first moment of the defect-link projector gradient:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta
=
\frac{\int d\xi\;\xi\,(\theta'(\xi))^2}{\int d\xi\;(\theta'(\xi))^2}.
}
\end{equation}

Thus a symmetric defect profile gives \(\delta=0\), while an asymmetric profile gives \(\delta\neq0\).

For the minimal asymmetric defect profile
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta'(\xi)
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}\sigma_d}
e^{-\xi^2/(2\sigma_d^2)}
\left(1+\epsilon \frac{\xi}{\sigma_d}\right),
}
\end{equation}
one finds exactly
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta
=
\frac{2\epsilon \sigma_d^2}{1+\epsilon^2/2}
\approx 2\epsilon \sigma_d^2
\qquad (|\epsilon|\ll 1).
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{4.6. Final Cabibbo formula}

Therefore the microscopic Cabibbo source is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\tan 2\theta_C
\sim
\frac{
2(\alpha_X\beta_X/M_X^2)\,
m_{12}^{\rm eff}\,
|\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)|
}{
\Delta H_{12}^{\rm diag}
},
}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)\propto \delta e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)},
\qquad
\delta\propto \epsilon \sigma_d^2.
\]

So, at leading structural level,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_C
\propto
\epsilon\,\sigma_d^2\,e^{-d^2/(4\sigma^2)}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{4.7. Honest status}

What is rigorous:
\begin{itemize}
\item midpoint-centered odd kernel gives zero,
\item nonzero Cabibbo requires parity breaking,
\item minimal offset model gives an exact closed form.
\end{itemize}

What remains open:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\epsilon \text{ still has to be computed quantitatively from the actual PDE branch.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Part V. Full CKM Structure and the Honest Boundary of the Current Derivation}

\subsection{5.1. Leading structural decomposition}

The current reduced TECT flavor picture is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{cb},V_{ub}
\;\text{are controlled mainly by geometry + sector misalignment,}
}
\end{equation}
while
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{us}
\;\text{is controlled mainly by the mixed }JK\text{ channel with a real parity-breaking datum.}
}
\end{equation}

This gives a natural split origin of the CKM hierarchy.

\subsection{5.2. Hierarchy logic}

The full hierarchy is then understood as follows:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(V_{us}\): nearest-family link, enhanced by \(JK\),
\item \(V_{cb}\): third-family dominant sector misalignment,
\item \(V_{ub}\): longer-range overlap plus stronger exponential suppression.
\end{itemize}

So the qualitative hierarchy
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|V_{us}| \gg |V_{cb}| \gg |V_{ub}|
}
\end{equation}
is structurally compatible with the TECT reduced mechanism.

\subsection{5.3. CP phase}

The complex phase source is naturally attributed to the internal \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) Berry structure:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal A_i = i z^\dagger \partial_i z.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore a complex phase in the mixed overlap
\[
\mathcal I_{AB}^{JK}
=
|\mathcal I_{AB}^{JK}|e^{i\varphi_{AB}}
\]
is geometrically natural.

At the structural level this supports
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta_{\mathrm{CKM}}
=
\arg\!\big[(H_d)_{12}(H_d)_{23}(H_d)_{31}\big]
}
\end{equation}
as a Berry-phase-type quantity.

\subsection{5.4. What is honestly closed}

The following is already closed at the structural level:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The common-kernel branch fails.}
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{A common skeleton plus sector-dependent shift naturally repairs }V_{cb},V_{ub}.
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Cabibbo mixing arises from the mixed }JK\text{ channel only after real parity breaking.}
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The CKM hierarchy has a split microscopic origin rather than a single ad hoc texture.}
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{5.5. What is still open}

The remaining finite branch-level tasks are:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(i) compute } \eta_A \text{ from the actual Class II background,}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(ii) compute } \epsilon \text{ from the actual asymmetric defect mode,}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(iii) compute the complex phase of }\mathcal I_{AB}^{JK}\text{ from the actual Berry/projector background.}
}
\end{equation}

Only after these are evaluated on a chosen branch does the CKM sector become genuinely parameter-free.

\subsection{5.6. Honest endpoint}

The strongest honest statement at present is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT provides a finite and structurally coherent microscopic mechanism for the full CKM pattern.}
}
\end{equation}

But equally importantly:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the final numerical closure still requires explicit branch-level extraction of } \eta_A,\ \epsilon,\ \text{and the Berry phase data.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The TECT flavor/CKM mechanism can be organized into five logically distinct parts.

First, the common-kernel branch
\[
Y_u=ED_u,\qquad Y_d=ED_d
\]
fails structurally because it keeps the left-handed up/down sectors too aligned.

Second, the correct geometric decomposition is
\[
q_A^{(s)}=c_A+\delta q_A^{(s)},
\]
with a common defect skeleton \(c_A\) and sector-dependent shifts
\[
\delta q_A^{(s)}=H_A^{-1}b_A^{(s)}.
\]
The actual force is neighbor-induced rather than self-induced.

Third, the nontrivial \(2\text{--}3\) and \(1\text{--}3\) CKM structure is naturally generated by geometric sector misalignment, especially when the third-family drift dominates.

Fourth, the Cabibbo angle is generated by the mixed \(JK\) channel only after a genuine parity-breaking datum is present.
For the minimal offset-kernel Gaussian model, the exact overlap is
\[
\mathcal I_{12}^{JK}(\delta)
=
\mathcal N_{JK}\,
\frac{4\xi^2\delta}{(2\xi^2+\sigma^2)^2}
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{d^2}{4\sigma^2}
-\frac{\delta^2}{2\xi^2+\sigma^2}
\right],
\]
and the offset itself is the first moment of the defect-link projector gradient:
\[
\delta
=
\frac{\int d\xi\;\xi\,(\theta'(\xi))^2}{\int d\xi\;(\theta'(\xi))^2}.
\]

Fifth, the full CKM hierarchy is most naturally understood as a split-origin structure:
\[
V_{cb},V_{ub}
\text{ are controlled mainly by geometry + sector misalignment,}
\]
while
\[
V_{us}
\text{ is controlled mainly by the mixed }JK\text{ channel with real parity breaking.}
\]

The honest present status is therefore:
\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT already supplies a coherent microscopic flavor mechanism,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{the final parameter-free numerical closure still requires explicit branch-level extraction of } \eta_A,\ \epsilon,\ \text{and the Berry-phase data.}
}
\]
